# Complete Guide To R Wrangling Visualizing And Modeling Data

> Trying to locate meaning and direction in big data is difficult. R can help you find your way. R is a statistical programming language to analyze and visualize the relationships between large amounts of data. This course with data analytics expert Barton Poulson provides a thorough introduction to R, with detailed instruction for installing and navigating R and RStudio and hands-on examples, from 

> [LinkedIn Learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data) | Intermediate | 30K learners
> [Jump to Path Context ↓](#path-context)

#### [Make your data make sense](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/make-your-data-make-sense?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/make-your-data-make-sense?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] We live in a noisy world, and trying to find some meaning and direction, that is, the signal in the noise, is never easy, but learning to work with data can help you hone in on the signal. I'm Barton Poulson, and in this course we'll look at how you can use the free open-source programming language R to find the signal in your data. This is an introductory course, so you don't need to have experience with R or computer programming. It's helpful but not necessary to have some familiarity with the basic concepts of [[Statistical Analysis]]. But either way, I'll explain each step thoroughly as we go. You'll see the power and the flexibility of R and how it can help you make the most of the data that's all around you, and that can help you find the vital signal in the universal noise. And so let's get started with the Complete Guide to R.

When you open this exercise files folder, you'll see that there are three sub folders. One is for all of the code that we'll be using in this course. A second one is for data sets that contain several files in multiple formats, as well as code books that explain where the data sets come from and what they all mean. The third one is output, which can contain any graphs or other output that you create. In addition to those, there are two other files. One is a README file that explains these folders and files. And another is a Our Project file, which helps us organize the documents and the software that we'll use. If you don't have access to the exercise files, that's okay. You can still follow along by watching how I use the course files.

### 1. What Is R?

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [R in context](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/r-in-context?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/r-in-context?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Any programming language or application is first and foremost a tool. And the general rule with tools is that you want to use the right tool for the job. That's true working in [[Data Science]] as well. You have a range of tools available for working with data, one of which is R. But there are other tools too, and I want to show you where R should be in your data science toolbox. The first and most obvious tool for working with data is a spreadsheet. I like to think of these as the universal data containers because they're everywhere and everybody uses them. Now, obviously, the common choices for spreadsheets include [[Microsoft Excel]] and [[Google]] Sheets, although there are many other possible choices. The reason spreadsheets are great tools is because they let you organize your data however you want. And they can sort and filter data, they can count and summarize data, and they can quickly make relatively sophisticated graphs. Truthfully, spreadsheets are probably sufficient for the majority of real world data tasks that don't involve creating models for your data. But when it's time to move beyond summaries and basic graphs and start making those statistical models, then you'll need to get a more sophisticated tool out of your toolbox, like a statistical application. Some of the most common statistical applications are SPSS and [[SaaS]], and my personal favorite, Jamovi, the open source application. All of these give user-friendly point and click interfaces
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/r-in-context?u=76281980&t=96)** for data exploration and modeling. But you may have data that doesn't fit nicely into the rows and columns that standard statistical applications expect. Or you may have questions that go beyond the procedures that are available in the dropdown menus. In either of those cases, you'll need to add something else to your toolbox. You'll need to take the final step to a data-oriented programming language, which gives you the ultimate in control and power in analyzing your data. Some of the most common and interesting choices for data-oriented programming are [[Python (Programming Language)|Python]], a powerful general purpose programming language that's been well adapted for working with data, Julia is an intriguing newcomer in scientific computing, and R is a language that was specifically developed for working with data. And of course, it's the subject of this course. Now, I want to give you a little bit of information about the relative popularity of some of these languages by using the KDnuggets poll of [[Data Mining]] professionals from 2019. This is in terms of how often they use tools when working with data. And obviously, you'll see right here that Python is the top of the list, where about two thirds of the respondent said it was their most common tool. Interestingly, the application RapidMiner was unusually high this year, that seems to have been the result of the poll being advertised on RapidMiner user groups.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/r-in-context?u=76281980&t=190)** But R's right here, about 50% report using R on a daily basis. I do want to point out that next after that is [[Microsoft Excel|Excel]]. Again, the spreadsheet is a universal data tool, and even with professional data mining and data science analysts, Excel's an important tool. Now in terms of data science jobs posted on Indeed, and this data is from 2017, so things may have changed a little bit since then, again, Python is at the top and R is here fifth on the list. It still represents about 15,000 jobs available on Indeed. And so it's an important part of any would-be professional data scientists toolbox when applying for jobs. And then in terms of scholarly articles, well, then what you see is that the application SPSS is still by far the most common in academic research. That specifically means research in people in fields like psychology and social work and education and business, where this is still the preferred tool of many of my colleagues in psychology. But R is second place on that one. Please note, Python doesn't even show up on this list because Python is generally used in computer science. And while computer science is an important academic topic, there are other ones that are more common like biology and chemistry. Now, I do want to give a quick answer to a question that always pops up in data science, and that is, should I learn Python or R?
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/r-in-context?u=76281980&t=283)** Well, you know, this isn't a really helpful debate, and I'll tell you why. It's because both of these have very important strengths. Python is especially strong in machine learning and database app development. Machine learning, [[Artificial Intelligence (AI)|artificial intelligence]] have become enormously important topics in the data world in the last few years. Artificial intelligence didn't even exist as a major subfield say five years ago within the data science community, but it has exploded with the development of [[Neural Networks]] and the availability of large, complex data sets. On the other hand, R is especially strong in data analysis that's different from machine learning and also in scientific research. Again, think biology, astronomy, and the social sciences. And so really, when we start thinking about, should you learn Python or R, well, part of it's going to depend on where you're coming from. If you're in the computer science world, you probably already know Python is easy to adapt. If you're trained as a researcher in a different field and you acquire a tool to analyze your data, R might be a common choice, but I think of it a little bit like the business world where, you know, nobody would ever say, "Should I advertise my business on [[Facebook]] or on [[Instagram]]?" The obvious answer is you're going to use both and many other platforms at the same time. That connects us to my final point, any professional data scientists, if you're going to work in the field, you're going to be expected to be able to work with many different tools,
>
> **[6:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/r-in-context?u=76281980&t=376)** including R and Python and [[Java]], and C++ and [[SQL]]. There are so many. There's a huge advantage to learning R. It's a major tool for many companies, for many fields, and it's often the tool of choice for common data tasks. It's a great way to work with data. It has a great community. And it will get you started on your path towards meaningful, productive work in data science.

#### [Data science with R: A case study](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/data-science-with-r-a-case-study?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/data-science-with-r-a-case-study?u=76281980&t=0)** - I want to start by giving you an idea of some of the things that you're able to do with R, specifically in terms of wrangling data and visualizing data. And so, I'm going to use a case study. This is based on actual data, and I'll run through a lot of the steps, a lot of the procedures that I cover in this course, not every single one of them, but many of them in terms of getting data organized and using visualizations to get insight into what's happening in the data. Now, I'll be moving through most of this pretty quickly because I'm going to be covering the details in later videos. But first, I'm going to load a series of packages that give me extra functionality for working in R. I'm going to use a dataset from the Product Plots Package called Happy, and I'm going to get a little bit of information about it. It's information on happiness from the general social survey, and it's a large dataset. It has over 50,000 observations, and we can see the names that are in it. These are the variables. And if you want more information, you can go to these websites. I had to use link shorteners to get them in. I'm going to start by saving the happy data into an object called DF for dataframe, save it as a tibble, and we'll get a quick look at it. We have id, happy, year, marital status, degree, financial relative to others, health, and a weight-in variable. Now, it turns out that we don't really need the ID or the weight-in variable.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/data-science-with-r-a-case-study?u=76281980&t=92)** Other research has shown the weight-in variable doesn't make much of a difference, so I'm going to get rid of those and select everything that's in between. So I'm going to replace my DF with a slightly more selective group. And my main outcome here is happy. How happy do people say they are? So let's take a look at what the choices are. They're "not too happy," "pretty happy," and "very happy," subjectively defined in self-report. Now, I'm going to flip around these values so that "very happy" comes first, and that'll put it at the top of some of the graphs I'm going to do. And now when we check that again, we see that it starts with a "very happy" and goes to "not too happy." Now, let's move on to exploring what the actual happiness variable looks like. It's a factor. It only has three categories, so the best way to do this is with a bar chart. So I'm going to do a bar chart using ggplot 2. And when we [[Zoom]] in on that, we see that we have a fair number of people who say they're "very happy," a larger number who say they're "pretty happy," a smaller number who say they're "not too happy," and a fair number who did not answer the question. Now, I don't want the people who didn't answer the question in the dataset because that's my main outcome and I can't model them, so I'm going to exclude those cases and then we'll get frequencies for happy. And there's the actual numbers that go with those bars. Now, let's start looking at some of the predictors. The first one I'm going to use just in the order that they appear is gender. And I'm going to do this by making a bar chart of the responses that people gave for sex.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/data-science-with-r-a-case-study?u=76281980&t=187)** And we have male here, we have slightly more female respondents. We'll get the frequencies, those are down here, 20,000 and nearly 26,000. And then we'll use 100% stacked bar chart, which is a good way of looking at the relationship between categories in two different groups. So I'm going to run that one, and then we'll zoom in on it. And we have male respondents on the left, female respondents on the right. "Very happy" is here at the top, green is "pretty happy," blue is "not too happy." And the proportions look nearly identical for the two groups. So let's say that there's appears to be no gender difference here in terms of happiness in this particular survey. So now let's look at marital status, another one that shows up. I'm going to get a bar chart for that one, and we can see that most of the respondents said that they were married, then we have never married, divorced, widowed, separated, and a small number who didn't answer the question. We'll get the frequencies for each of those. So we have over 25,000 married. Then we'll do 100% stacked bar chart for this variable, and I'm going to exclude the people who didn't respond. When we get that, we can see that there seems to be a big difference here, that the people who were married, that's here on the left, seemed to have a much higher percentage who say they were "very happy." And then we look at people who were never married, divorced, widowed, and separated, we see the number that are "not too happy" increases. That suggests that we may be able to collapse this variable and make a dichotomous married versus not married variable.
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/data-science-with-r-a-case-study?u=76281980&t=283)** I'm going to do that with this command that uses mutate and we'll get the frequencies for that. I have 20,000 not married about, I get the same, nearly 26,000 in married. And we'll look at the stacked bar chart now. This is just for the two groups. The "not married" are on the left, and you see that the "not too happy" is bigger than the "married" on the right. And the "married" say that they are "very happy," a larger percentage than those who are not. And so, there may be an effect or relationship between relationships and happiness. Now, level of education, I'm going to look at that one. I see that a lot of people in this dataset reported finishing at high school. We'll look at the frequencies, it got some numbers over there. And the 100% stacked bar chart. And what we have on this one is in terms of "not too happy," people with less than high school seem to be not too happy. The number of "very happy" doesn't seem to change a whole lot, but we can create a dichotomous variable that simply says whether they went to college or not. So that'll be these top three groups, junior college, bachelor, and graduate. I'll run that command right here. We'll look at the frequencies. We have about 11,000 who went to college of some form and 35,000 who didn't. And then we'll get a stacked bar chart between the two groups. I'll zoom in on that. And the difference is not as big as when we look at just the people who didn't finish high school, but there is a possible association here. Again, with the people who graduated from college,
>
> **[6:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/data-science-with-r-a-case-study?u=76281980&t=376)** that's on the right, a slightly larger percentage say that they are very happy, and the people who never went to college, a slightly higher percentage say that they're not too happy. Now let's look at financial status, which, of course, is associated with education. We'll get a bar chart for the financial status groups, and I'll zoom in on that. What you see is far below average, below average, most of people say they're average, smaller number above average, and a very small number far above average. It's practically a bell curve, what we're looking at here. And let's get the frequencies that go with that. There we have the actual numbers. And let's get a stacked bar chart to look at how those are connected to happiness. And when I zoom in on that, we can see there's a pretty linear relationship. As finances go up, the proportionate people who say they're "very happy" seems to increase. The most noticeable thing is that people who are "not too happy," the below average, is high, and the far below average is very high. This is consistent with the research that says that money is good for getting people out of misery. It doesn't usually make them happy, but it's good for getting them out of misery. Now, if we want to, we can dichotomize this variable to make it so whether people have average or above finances. We can get the frequencies for that, and then get a stacked bar chart for that as well. And when we do that, the relationship's a little clearer. People who have at least average finances are much more likely to say that they're "very happy"
>
> **[7:51](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/data-science-with-r-a-case-study?u=76281980&t=471)** and people who have inadequate finances are much more likely to say they're "not too happy." Now, for health. Again, this is self-reported. And what we have in this one is on the far left are people who say that they're in poor health, then fair health, then good health, then excellent health, and then a lot of people did not respond to this question. That's the last bar in gray. Let's get the actual frequencies, and then let's get a stacked bar chart that looks at the proportions for each group. When we do that, we see that people who say they're in excellent health, well, they tend to say that they're "very happy" and we see the opposite pattern with people who are in poor health, much more likely to say they're "not too happy" now. And so this, again, is another potential factor for predicting or trying to understand why people say they're happy or not. Now, we could also look at a few temporal variables. There's the year of the survey, 'cause this was administered over many different years. This is a histogram, and we see going from about 1970 up to the 2000s, and we can get descriptive [[Statistics]] for those. So for instance, we see that the meeting was 1988, the earliest was 1972. We can look to see if there's an association between level of happiness and the year of the survey. Now, this one's a little funny to read. We have "very happy" here, "pretty happy," "not too happy." They look mostly similar to me. We could also do boxplots to look at the associations between those. There's a lot of overlap, no obvious differences.
>
> **[9:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/data-science-with-r-a-case-study?u=76281980&t=565)** On the other hand, age may be associated with happiness. So let's look at a histogram of age. And we see that we have most of our people are relatively young, and then it just tapers down until you get to the oldest group. We can get the descriptive statistics for that. We have people going from 18, a median of 42, and a maximum of 89 in this particular sample. Now, we could create age groups, generation groups, but we don't need to do that here. We'll look at the density plots of happiness by age again. And what's interesting in this one is while they do seem to be pretty similar, there seems to be a flattening out or a bump here in the "very happy" group going from about 55 years old to about 70. It looks like there's slightly more people in that age group who say they're very happy than we would expect based on the other two groups. We can also do boxplots, but when we do that, we don't see the same effect. They are largely overlapping. We can look at the year born. Now, to get this, we simply have to take the year that the survey was administered and subtract their age at that time. That'll give us an approximation of the year that they're born. We'll get a histogram. And what you see on this is we got people who were born a long time ago. If we go to descriptives, we're going back to 1883 in this particular sample. Those would be people who were very old in 1972 in the first round of the survey. We can get density plots and see if it looks like there's any obvious association between the two. Those all look pretty similar and the same with boxplots, but that's a quick run-through the data.
>
> **[11:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/data-science-with-r-a-case-study?u=76281980&t=660)** Again, the point here is not to reach final conclusions, but to raise questions and to guide us for further investigation. That's the whole point of visualization and wrangling the data, putting it into the shape that's going to be most useful. From this, we've got some clues. And in part two of this course, a separate course where we talk about analysis and modeling, I'll take a closer look at these and actually build statistical models to use these variables to predict happiness. But for right now, what this does is it gives you a good idea of some of the things that you can learn in this course in terms of organizing your data, helping it to get into the form that's best suited for answering your questions, and getting the visualizations that give you the insight and the ideas for your analysis.

#### [Data types and structures](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/data-types-and-structures?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/data-types-and-structures?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The same number can mean different things in different contexts depending on what the data type is and also the structure that it's in. And even though you don't have to declare a variable type, when you first enter things in R, you have an enormous amount of control over what your data means and how it is used. Let's start by going and looking at some of the fundamental data types in R. The most common is numeric. And so I'm going to create a variable here called n1 for numeric number one, and I'm going to assign a value of 15. Now the thing I want you to notice is that this is an integer number. I didn't say 15 point anything, but when I ask to see it, I'll just call n1. There's my 15. And then I use the command typeof, I'm asking R to tell me what type it is. And you'll notice it does not say that it's integer. It says that it's double precision, so it's acting like it has lots and lots of decimal places even though it knows that they're all zeros. So this is the default type. Now you can do integers, and I'm going to show you how to do that in a minute. But let's take a look at this one. I'm going to do n2 for numeric two, and I'm going to save the value of 1.5. And we bring that up to the console and you see that it also is double with its one decimal place, okay? So that tells us that numeric values are by default double precision. So it's prepared as though there were a need for many decimal places. You can also do character variables.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/data-types-and-structures?u=76281980&t=94)** So here I'm going to do c1 for character one, and I'm going to save the letter "c" in it. And when I save that, you can see it's displayed over here. It's got its quotes around it. I'll have it come down to the console, and there it is. But the typeof is character also. R does not distinguish between a single character and a collection of characters. Other variables, we'll call those char or character and string. But in R, they're both character variables. Now please notice in all caps it needs to be in all caps and it does not have quotes around it because it's not a character, it's a logical variable. Now there are [[Data Structures]], so obviously just a single number, single data point is not going to be too helpful all on its own when you're doing your analysis. There's a lot of situations where a single vector is going to be really important. That's a one dimensional collection of numbers, either a single row or maybe a single column.

> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/data-types-and-structures?u=76281980&t=281)** What's more common, however, is that you need rows and columns and the fundamental type there is a matrix. > **[9:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/data-types-and-structures?u=76281980&t=561)** and we're going to get the display of that one. Now I want to finish by talking about coercing, which in the real world is a bad thing, in the data world, it simply means converting a variable from one data type or structure to another. Now there's what's called automatic origin. This is where R automatically goes to the least restrictive data type. So for instance, here I have a 1, character b, and a logical value. Now as we saw before, that's going to go and switch all of them to characters, 'cause of those three that's the least restrictive. And I say typeof, and it says they're all characters. Let's try coercing to integer. Now I showed you earlier that for numeric values, double precision for lots of decimal places the standard, but you can specify integer. And now we're going to get it and then call it up. It now recognizes it as an integer with no decimal places.

> **[10:56](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/data-types-and-structures?u=76281980&t=656)** It also may be that you have data that has character values, but they're numbers and you want to treat them as numbers. And even though they look the same, they have a different structure. So all I'm going to do here is I'm going to add this as.data.frame command. And when I do that, you can see it displays very differently. This one has the row indexes and column indexes. This one has the variable names, which it went to a default v1, 2, 3 for variable 1, 2, 3. And it has the row numbers, but it makes it possible to do a lot of different analyses that we couldn't do otherwise. So this is a lot to say that R gives you flexibility, both in terms of how you define your data and its types, how you structure it, and then moving things back and forth to suit your purposes to get the meaning you need out of your data.

#### [Comments and headers](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/comments-and-headers?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/comments-and-headers?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] If you're really highly motivated, you can write your command in R just as one single long, massive line using cryptic function names and shortcuts. And really nobody has any idea what's going on. A better way is to organize your scripts, your programming, so you know what's happening. So it's easy to find your way through it, and so it's easy to share it with other people. Two related approaches, using comments and using section headers can facilitate both of those. First thing is the comment character in R is the pound sign or number sign or octatherp if you want to use a typographical term. And that tells our don't run what comes after this. So at the top of each script, I've got this little bit of [[Metadata]] that gives the title, the file, and the project, and I've got that pound sign in front each of them to say don't run this. Then I can use that to both describe what's happening. So for instance, down here, I say that this next command load base packages manually, and I say specifically is for the example data sets. Sometimes you put it above to give just a brief description. You can put it afterwards also. In addition to explaining code so you know what's going on, one of the neat things about comments is it allows you to temporarily turn off and on code. So let me do this. >
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/comments-and-headers?u=76281980&t=95)** and let's take a look at the first few lines. But watch what happens right here. I'm going to make a histogram and I'm going to do this by running this command. And you see I have two lines commented out. I've got this command that currently is commented out, so R just ignores it like it's not there, but I can put it back in. Within R studio, you can have three levels of comments and you put a pound sign and then a space, and then the name of the header and then at least four characters after it. If you want to have a level two, you put two comment signs or two pound signs, a space, then the name. Or for a third level heading, you do three. And you can follow it up with either more pound signs, which is what I use for level header or with equal signs, which is what I use for level two, or with dashes, which is what I use for level three. I also change the capitalization from all caps to title caps to sentence capitalization because that's what's actually used within my field in psychology for research manuscripts. But what's neat about this is we come back over here to say show the document outline, now you can see those headers. Now it's unfortunate that they're not indented. That would be helpful, but it is a quick and easy way to bounce around. You can do it there and you can do it right here. And when you set it up that way, it makes it easy for you to organize your code

> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/comments-and-headers?u=76281980&t=281)** to break off and distinguish sections in the code by their function, and then use comments to explain what's happening and then to turn on or off elements of your code so you can try experimenting with what's going to be most useful in your work in R.

In fact, if you go to the comprehensive R Archive Network for packages and you search by name, these are all the packages that have tidy in their name because they're playing off of what was found to be such a significant way to work with data. #### [Piping commands with %>%](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/4584221?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/4584221?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Sometimes code can be difficult and this can be as true for R as anything else. And one of the places this is most apparent is with nested commands. So for example, here's a command that I actually used in some work that I was doing, and it's a single, really long statement. And looking at it, it's extremely hard to tell what's happening, what order it's happening, and how things relate to each other. It's a nightmare. I mean, what we're doing is we're starting with a dataset called UCBAdmissions. And then from that, we're going to get a margin table. But I have to have the three to argument for it on the other side of the dataset. And then I want to turn it into proportions and then I want to round it off, but round the command is way over at the left. And the number of decimal places I want is all the way on the other side of the page is a two and then I want to turn it into values out of 100. And so it starts in the middle, it goes out on each side, but it's in a really difficult to follow order. Fortunately, you don't have to keep it that way. Pipes come to the rescue. Many programming languages involve the concept of pipes, which lets you take the output of one command and pipe it or feed it to the input of another command. Now in R, there are two versions of pipes available. The first that's been around the longest is called the magrittr pipe. It's a play on the name of Rene Magritte, the French painter
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/4584221?u=76281980&t=96)** who had a painting that said, "This is not a pipe." And the magrittr pipe, which is part of the tidyverse and installed with the tidyverse packages that we use is the percent sign, then a greater than sign, then a percent sign. It's a little funny looking, but it's a way of wrapping a function in R. And there's a shortcut for it and you get used to doing it. Now the magrittr pipe is pretty flexible. There are other versions of it. So for instance, there's the one that has the less than and greater than, that's called the compound assignment pipe. It's when you're both modifying the data and overriding the existing data, I use it sometimes. And there's the capital T pipe also called the T pipe when you're taking the output and sending it into two different commands. Again, I use it on occasion. The magrittr pipe also uses something called a dot operator, which lets you put the output as not necessarily the first argument in the following command, but somewhere else. And you indicate that with a dot that says, put the output here. For that reason, the magrittr pipe is very flexible and it's the one that I use in this course. Then again, more recently, R finally developed their own native version of a pipe, and it's the vertical pipe and the greater than sign. It's nice 'cause it's built in and you don't have to install a package, although magrittr just comes with the tidyverse. It's less flexible, but that's by design. It simply takes the command and forwards it. You don't have compound assignment,
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/4584221?u=76281980&t=189)** you don't have the T pipe, and you don't have the dot operator. But again, that can be seen as a feature 'cause it keeps things clearer and more concise. But in this course, I'm using the magrittr pipe and let me show you how that works with the example of the code I gave you a moment ago. So let's go back to my original command. This long confusing inside to outside nightmare. Now let's rewrite it using pipes. We start by saying, use the UCBAdmissions dataset. And then I have the pipe character. And then below that and slightly indented, I say, and I want a margin table of it. And then I'm able to put the three, which is the argument for which margin I want right there. So it's very clear what argument goes with what function. Then we take that output and pipe it to the next command, which is the prop.table for proportion table. And the empty parentheses are to indicate that it's a function, but I'm not adding any additional arguments. And then we take that and we feed it to the next command, which is round to round off the number of decimal places. And I'm able to put the argument to right there. And then when we get all done, I can use a special function that is multiply by and then 100. This is really easy to read. I personally find the vertical orientation much easier to follow and the command and argument right next to each other, and it goes first this, then this, then this. And finally, this is so much easier to read
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/4584221?u=76281980&t=283)** and actually makes it easier to write the code too. Now, in general, you can think of it as this. You have a function that you're applying to data. With a pipe, you can put it as data or the argument that goes in the parentheses is piped into the function. Or more generically, you can take a function with some data and an argument, and then you take the data and then pipe it into the function with an argument like, for instance, the number of decimal places you want. And when you have several functions and several arguments, instead of having this start in the middle and go out mess, you get to say, start with the data, pipe it to the first function with its argument, the second function with its argument, and the third function with its argument. And that is what makes life so much easier when dealing with your code. Easier to follow, easier to write, easier to troubleshoot, easier to deal with.

> [!info]- Semantic Content

The thing is, you don't have to do that every time. Once you get your data in there, you have an option of saving it as a native R data format. Now, there's a couple of reasons you might want to do that. Number one is you don't have to go through all the transformations again, which makes it easier and less error prone. #### [Introduction to ggplot2](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/introduction-to-ggplot2?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/introduction-to-ggplot2?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] One of the things you find when you're using R is there's more to R than just R. Specifically in this course, I've been using things like the Tidyverse, a whole collection of packages that not only extend the functionality of R, but actually change the way that you interact with it. In addition, part of the Tidyverse, ggplot2 represents a profoundly different way of creating graphics, and has become, for many people, the gold standard of R graphics. And I want to introduce you a little bit to the theory behind ggplot2. The first thing is the very peculiar name. Ggplot2, the gg part comes from, grammar of graphics. It's inspired by this book, "The Grammar of Graphics," second edition by Leland Wilkinson. This is the basis of the theory of the grammar of graphics and ggplot and ggplot2 are implementations of that grammar of graphics. The basic idea here is to separate what is graphed, that is the actual data behind it, from how it is graphed or the way that it is represented in the graphic. You can see this when you look at the abstract general structure of ggplot2 commands. They don't all include all of these. They can be very short, but they give you the possibility of addressing a layered grammar of graphics from several different elements. So for instance, here at the top, where you call ggplot, you actually specify what data is going into it, and you may actually have some commands
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/introduction-to-ggplot2?u=76281980&t=93)** that go there in the data statement. Then you go to the GEOM function, where GEOM stands for geometric or geometric object. It can be a histogram, it can be a dot, it can be a line. It can actually be very sophisticated things. But you take that one function and then you start telling it things like the mapping. How are you going to map the AES, stands for aesthetic elements, the actual visual things that are depicted, that represent the data. You can also do certain statistical transformations right here in terms of how you show the data, and you can adjust the position of objects to best match the goals you have in your graphics. After that, you can also specify coordinate functions. Say for instance, you want to do a polar coordinate system. You could specify that here, and you can also have a FACET function, which allows you to include multiple graphs, possibly in rows and columns to get a broader perspective on what you're working with. Now, ggplot2 also includes something called qplot, which stands for quick plot. These are commands that are quicker to work with. They're easy, they're fast, but they do have less power and control. I use them, and when I'm trying to do something where I don't feel a need to modify anything, I'll use a qplot command and I'll demonstrate them several times in this course. Now I want to give you a few other resources for ggplot2. One is the actual ggplot2 page on [tidyverse.org](https://tidyverse.org), which explains a little bit about how
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/introduction-to-ggplot2?u=76281980&t=189)** to install it and gives a link to some other information. One thing you might want to look at is this page, which is ggplot2 extensions. These are other packages that build onto and connect with the functionality of ggplot. They allow you to do some impressive things like animations or simple things like modifying where the labels appear. There are so many possibilities, and obviously, this is where you can see the power of ggplot because it lets you specify things at such a micro level. It enables enormous creativity in the exploration and the presentation of your data. Finally, I want you to be aware of the cheat sheets that are available through our studio because the people who've developed ggplot2, Hadley Wickham in particular, works at our studio. This is a downloadable PDF, which can give you a list of commands, including the over 40 different geometric objects and how you can specify some of the commands for working in ggplot. So these are resources that are available to you, but in the videos that follow, I'll be showing you some very simple commands, both with qplot, ggplot as ways of exploring data that are consistent both with the Tidyverse approach to R and all of which work together to help you better or explore, understand and present your data.

Say for instance, we can get an example by looking at this one, BuPu, and ask for seven different colors. When I run that command, here, we go from sort of blue to purple. So that's sequential. You can tell you're getting more and more of something. Diverging means you started a central value and you're indicating sort of negative values and positive values. So here's a five color diverging one. And then qualitative is when you're simply trying to indicate different groups or categories. So here's one with four that you can easily tell apart. To use the R Color Brewer palettes in your graphs, all you need to do is say col for color and then specify brewer.pal, which is short for palette,
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/using-color-palettes?u=76281980&t=284)** and then how many colors you want, and then the name of the palette. It's a short name. So let's use this first one, a sequential palette. And then here is a diverging palette. And then here is the qualitative palette. And so any of these, and you've got a lot of choices, can be a great way of adding a little more interest to your graphs, as well as guiding the eye and making it easier to see what's happening. Well, R Color Brewer is probably the most popular color palette package for R. There are others. One that I like 'cause I think it's amusing, is Wes Anderson. And if we click on this link, you'll see that Wes Anderson is named after the filmmaker, Wes Anderson, who's made a number of movies like "Bottle Rocket" and the "Royal Tenenbaums" and "Rushmore." In fact, now if we come back here, we can load the package. You get some information, it just tells you that it exists. But let's see the names of the palettes. There are all the palettes. From BottleRocket through IsleofDogs. And if you want to use those, you just use wes_palette and then the name of the palette and the color command. So here, we have the BottleRocket, and Zissou1, and GrandBudapest1, and IsleofDogs1. You'll see also that it repeats the colors because not many of these palettes have six colors in them, but that's what R does anytime you have a smaller number of colors compared to the number of things you're representing. Now, there are some other color palette options.
>
> **[6:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/using-color-palettes?u=76281980&t=378)** For instance, there's this one right here, which is really handy for getting palettes that work well with colorblindness, and for photocopying, and gray scale. You've got a number of choices right here. And then there's also a collection of scientific journal and sci-fi themed color palettes at this option. And when you open that, you see we have for The Lancet, and for JAMA, and the University of Chicago, we also have Star Trek, Tron, Futurama and Simpsons palettes. And so you have a number of options here. I want to finish by pointing out one other thing. Not only do you have these existing palettes, but maybe you work someplace that has specific color requirements. You have an organization, a company you work for, and they have their template. Well, it's very easy to specify a custom template. All you need to do is save the colors into an object in memory. For instance, I'm going to make one called palette1, and I'm just saving a list of the color names. I'm putting 'em together with C, which is for concatenate, and I can save those into memory. And you see my palette now shows up right here. Or you can use any other method that R gives you for indexing numbers like the hex codes. So here are the same colors in hex code, just in reversed order. And then simply call that palette under the color command. So here's the first palette, and then here it is flipped around in order. With that, you've got an enormous amount of control, both to get a cohesive presentation,
>
> **[7:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/using-color-palettes?u=76281980&t=473)** to draw attention and guide the eye, and hopefully, help both you and the people you're working with, see what's happening in your data, get some insight, and do something useful with it.

So when I do that, you can see now I've got a bell curve here. And so if you need to do bell curves or skew distributions, beta distributions, you got a lot of choices. #### [Creating box plots](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-box-plots?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-box-plots?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] After you make a histogram, probably the most helpful chart for a quantitative variable, something that you're counting or measuring, is a box plot because those are very helpful for both simplifying the data that you're showing, as well as checking for the presence of outliers, which can have an enormous influence on your analyses. To do this, let's load a few packages, pacman, and then the tidyverse and some others, and we're going to use the iris dataset. Now, I know I've been talking about this, but if you want some formal information on this, we do question mark iris, and it's Edgar Anderson's dataset, also well known as Fisher's dataset. And we have 150 rows of data, three different species of iris and four different measurements. But let's start by doing a box plot, and I'm going to use the base graphics first. I'm simply going to say take iris and give me a box plot. And when we do that, we get a funny kind of box plot 'cause one of these actually shouldn't be charted. What you find here is that we have over here a species, which is a text variable, but internally it's coded as one, two, and three. But from the [[Representational State Transfer (REST)|rest]] of these, you can see that sepal length is generally the longest. Sepal width, there's not a lot of variability there. There's a few outliers. Petal length is all over the place. Petal width is pretty low. This is a good way for your own personal exploratory graphics if you're doing the analysis. Now, you can also look at one variable at a time.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-box-plots?u=76281980&t=94)** All we have to do here is say, start with iris, select Petal.Length, and then may we flip it sideways. I actually prefer to do box plots left to right because then it's on the same scale as say a histogram. So let's run that one. And there we go. Very simple. You see, we got a really big box here in the middle, which tells us something peculiar is happening. And we have no outliers on the left or the right, but let's try breaking it down by groups. That's one of the important things about drilling down even just a little bit in your data to see what's happening. That means what I'm going to do here actually is not a box plot, it's going to be a dot plot. >
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-box-plots?u=76281980&t=187)** And it gives us dots for the individual measurements. All I have to do is add this argument, col for color is equal to species. When I run that, now you see they're all in the same place now that we just know which one is which by the color as well as the legend at the bottom. But now let's color them by group and let's add a box plot and let's jitter them so they're not on top of each other. So they'll be randomly moved a little bit to the left and to the right. When I do that, we get a graph that can look a little funny at first because they're spread out a fair amount to the left and to the right, but you get to see now what the overall distribution looks like 'cause a box plot shows you the range of the middle 50%, this is the median right here, and then the other lines go to the highest and lowest non-outlying points. Now let's remove the jittered points and look at just the box plots, which is how you would normally do this. And here we can zoom in and we see that we have these three groups and we have outliers marked separately. But otherwise, we have a very tight distribution here. And these ones overlap a little bit. To do this in ggplot, I'm just going to give you one example because we're really getting a lot of what we need just from a quick plot. I say take iris, feed it into ggplot, and what we're going to show is species where the outcome variable is Petal.Length and we're going to color it by species.
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-box-plots?u=76281980&t=281)** I say I want a box plot. We're going to do a cord flip. That means flip the coordinates. So instead of going up and down, it goes left to right. We'll take off the X label because we know what the species are, and we do not need a legend. When I run that, you see I have more control over what's going on here. We have virginica, versicolor, and setosa, and it's really easy to see what's happening. So a box plot, a great way of simplifying the display of a quantitative or measured variable, and doing it in base graphs or in qplot or in ggplot, any one of those, is going to give you the tools you need to start exploring your data and guiding your subsequent analyses.

#### [Creating scatterplots](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-scatterplots?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-scatterplots?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Univariate analyses or looking at one variable at a time are critical, they're informative, and they can be interesting. But really where things get exciting are when you look at associations between variables. And one of the most basic ways to do that is with scatter plots or ways of showing the relationship between two quantitative variables. Those are really easy to do in R. And we have both Qplot and ggplot versions to demonstrate here. I'm going to start by installing a few packages that's pacman, and then the tidyverse and so on. Let's come down here. Now, let's start by doing a basic scatter plot. And for this, I'm going to be using the iris dataset. We're going to look at the association between two of the measurements, the pedal width and the pedal length for the three species of irises. We're going to do a basic version, I call Qplot. Then I tell it the first variable, the second variable, and then I give it the name of the source. That's iris dataset. So let's run that one. And that shows up down here. And it's a basic scatterplot. We have the pedal width across the bottom, we have the length up the side, and we have a dot for each data point. There are about 150 dots here, and that's easy to do. On the other hand, you can tell there's something funny going on 'cause we've got this big gap here, but otherwise, it's kind of a nice linear association. But let's do this. We know that there are three species in this data set and they operate a little bit differently. So what I'm going to do now is I'm going to make one small modification to my Qplot command,
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-scatterplots?u=76281980&t=93)** aside from breaking it into lines where I'm going to say color = species. So color it by the species of the flower. When I run that command, we get this chart and now you can see that there's a big difference. We have one species of iris that's down here all by itself. That's the setos. The green are the versicolor, the blue are the virginica. And so you can see that there are really important differences between the species, even if the general pattern seems pretty consistent. Now that's Qplot, but let's try doing some of this with ggplot2 which gives us, again, some additional functionality. So what I'm going to do is I'm going to start with a basic scatter plot. Where I say ggplot, and then we're going to use the iris dataset. Then I say the aesthetics. What is it that I'm actually trying to show? And that is the pedal width and the pedal length. And then we're going to depict it using geom_point. That means put dots on my chart, make it a scatterplot. So we run that command and this is basically identical to what we had before with the Qplot. Now let's do something a little different. There's supposed to be 150 data points on that. And what we don't really see that many dots, it's because some of them are lying on top of each other. So we can jitter them as a way of separating the data points. And we do this by using instead of geom_point, which is what we had last time, geom or geometrical object jitter, which means still points, but now with a little bit of random up and down
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-scatterplots?u=76281980&t=185)** and left and right. Not enough to change the interpretation of the data, but enough that you can separate the data points. So let's run that command and let's [[Zoom]] in on that one for just a moment. Again, there's a small amount of random variation thrown into the data, but it actually helps you see the overall pattern better. And we still have this strong uphill pattern, but let's try doing a few other things. I'm going to make two modifications to this. I am both going to change the size of the points depending on the length of the sepal. That's another dimension. So a separate measurement. We're going to color it by species, and I'm also going to use the jitter points, but make them a little bit transparent by sending alpha. That is, you usually have red, green, blue, and alpha, which modulates the transparency. I'm going to set that to .5, it goes from zero to one. So let's run that command. And now when we zoom in on this, the size of the dot simply indicates the sepal length. That's a third measurement, but now you can see a little bit more about this association between things. And obviously we have big circles up here, which means sepal length is bigger there than it is down here, which isn't too surprising. Let's add a fit line, a regression line. And when we do that, we're going to do it separately for each of the categories. It draws a separate regression line
>
> **[4:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-scatterplots?u=76281980&t=278)** and a standard error for each of the groups independently. You can see it's basically uphill. It's a little stronger uphill for the green. That's the versicolor and the others. And that's what we did with smooth. This smooth is a way of going through there. And then lm stands for a linear model. And then let's do one more where we add onto it a density 2D. This is going to make it look like maps with circles drawn around them as a way of indicating something akin to confidence intervals, but really, a map to indicate how bunched up the data are. And this is as elaborate as we're going to get in this one, but let's take that and then zoom in. And now what you see are these ridges, like looking at a map of a mountains that look at the density of the data points. Obviously, they're densest here in the middle, but it does reach out to some of the outliers. And so again, this is an illustration of what you're able to do with ggplot2. And here, we're using it to look at the association between two quantitative variables, measurements of a petal width and length. We're able to break it down by the species. We're able to add some extra elements to help us better understand the relationship between the variables. And that is the huge advantage of using ggplot2.

#### [Creating cluster charts](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-cluster-charts?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-cluster-charts?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] When you have a number of different cases, different people, or different objects, or different companies, or clients, and you want to see how they group with one another. A cluster analysis is a great way to do this. It takes the variables in your dataset and looks for similarities of each case based on those variables. And you can either do a fixed number of clusters, you say, "I want five groups, and so make that happen." Or you can do what's called a hierarchical cluster where you say, "Start at zero, go down to individual cases or vice versa." And that gives you a little more flexibility in how you split it up. Now, I've demonstrated this in other courses, what's a little different here, and the reason I'm including it is not because ggplot has a special command for clustering. I'm going to use the standard clustering. But when you're working in the tidyverse and you have, for instance, tibbles for your data, a little bit of modifications necessary in how you set things up. So I want to demonstrate this. I'm going to start by loading my packages. And then let's come down here and we're going to use the MT cars dataset. This is data from MotorTrend Magazine in 1974. And if you want to see the actual dataset, we just do mtcars and let's run that one down. And here you see we have, you know, the Dodge Duster, the Plymouth Valiant, the Mazda RX4, with the Ford Pantera, the Maserati Bora, and we've got a bunch of information about them. Now, the important thing to remember about cluster analysis
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-cluster-charts?u=76281980&t=94)** is it can't give you some universal truth based on how similar things really are. It's always based on the actual data that you feed to it. So you have to remember, it's circumscribed by and contingent on the data that you provide. And even then, I'm not going to use all the data that we have right here, but let me show you what I'm going to do. I want to do one more thing, and I'm going to use the tidyverse command glimpse just so we can see what kind of variables. These are all double precision variables, even the ones that are written down as though they are categorical. Now, here's what I'm going to do, and this is why I am demonstrating this here is how to prepare the data. I'm going to feed it into df, which stands for data frame, and I just use that as a generic data name. We're going to start with mtcars, and then we're going to do row names to columns. And the reason we got to do that is 'cause the actual names of the cars here in this original data dataset, those are not a variable. Those are row names but when you go to a tibble, which is something that you use off in the tidyverse approach, you lose the row names. So we're going to put them into an explicit column, then we'll save it as a tibble, which makes certain things possible. Then I'm going to select a few variables that are important to me. I'm going to first take the new variable that's created by the ID, which becomes row name. I'm going to rename it as car. Then I'm going to select these other variables. So it's not all of 'em, but it's a lot of them. Then I'm going to do something that's really important for hierarchical clustering or any kind of clustering.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-cluster-charts?u=76281980&t=189)** I'm going to rescale the quantitative variables. The problem here is if you come down here, you know, we've got like number of cylinders, it's going from three to eight, but we have, displacement is in the hundreds, and weight is potentially in the thousands. We're on very different scales for these. And unfortunately, numbers that are on bigger scales have more influence when you do a cluster analysis. And so you want to rescale them. I'm going to be using the scale command, which means turn them into Z scores. It means redo the variables so that the mean is zero, the standard deviation is one, and it basically puts them all on the same scale. Right here, I'm saying don't do it for the name of the car because that's not a numeric variable. That would be silly. But standardize everything else using the mutate at command. And then we'll print it to see what it looks like when we run it. So it's saved over here in df. And when we come down to the bottom, we can see that we now have a tibble. We have the name of the car. Then we have these other variables that are basically all on the same scale now. So that's good. What I'm going to do now is analyze the data. I've got a few different choices for cluster analysis. The first one is whether you want to do what's called agglomerative or divisive. Agglomerative means that every observation starts out on its own and then they get joined into groups as you go up. So they are accreting, they're getting these bigger groups. Divisive means they all start together
>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-cluster-charts?u=76281980&t=282)** and then you start separating them out. Now, the fact is you tend to get similar results, but there are also a few other choices you can make. But I just want you to be aware, we're going to be using the approach called hclust. That's from the built-in [[Statistics]] functions. And it is agglomerative. So it starts out with every one of the cars on its own, and then it combines them until we end them all up together. So let's do this by getting the clusters. To do this, I'm going to create an object called hc, which stands for hierarchical clusters. We take dfr data, we calculate the distance or really a dissimilarity matrix. And then we use the hclust function, the agglomerative function to calculate the clusters. So I run that one and we've got this item that showed up over here. And now we can plot that. > **[7:52](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-cluster-charts?u=76281980&t=472)** in your data. Obviously, the way you set it up is going to change things a little bit, as will the data that you feed into it. But this is a great way of visualizing some potentially useful clusters in your data. And then you can think about whether it makes sense to treat the cases within each of these clusters as identical for a specific purpose to help you do something practical with your data. That's the point of cluster analysis in general. And this demonstration, my whole purpose was to show you some of the ways that you adapt, the way that you work with the data when you're using the tidyverse, and then feeding it into a cluster analysis.

### 5. Wrangling Data

#### [Creating tidy data](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-tidy-data?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-tidy-data?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] There's a general rule of thumb in the data world that 80% of the time on any data project is spent getting ready for things. And wrangling data, getting the data ready is a very big part of that. But getting your data into what's called a tidy format, having tidy data, is one way of organizing things that makes your life much, much easier. And so I want to give you a quick example of how that can work with several different kinds of untidy data. We'll start by loading a few packages with pacman first. And then we're going to load a few extra packages, including one called tsibble, T-S-I-B-B-L-E, and that is a tibble from the Tidyverse for time series data, as well as lubridate for working with dates and XML for working one kind of structured data, so let's load those. If you don't have those installed already, it might take just a moment. And then we're going to come down here, and let me give you the very short version of what tidy data means. Now this comes from a paper by Hadley Wickham, who's very well known in our community. The idea is that tidy data has a very specific and predictable structure for cells, rows, columns, and the files. So each column in your data addresses one variable, also called a field or an attribute. But it's one variable per column, a column is a variable, a variable is a column. Each row in your data is a case or observation. So each case gets one row, and each row gets one case.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-tidy-data?u=76281980&t=96)** Then at the meeting point of the columns and the rows are the cells. And that is a single value, encoded with text or numbers. And then the file that you have, is one level of observation or abstruction, so for instance, you might have addresses for customers. And that would go in one data set, and then you might have another one that says addresses of stores. That would be a different thing 'cause there are different levels of operation. But if you set things up this way, it becomes very easy to move your data from one program to another, and to share the data with others, and your code is going to work better with it, 'cause that's the format that it's expecting. Now, on the other hand, there's a lot of untidy data out there. There are spreadsheets which maybe got set up with merged cells and with formulas going between this page and that, or they have seven different things in the cell, or you have scraped data, and there's a lot of other things that are not tidy because they don't have the structured rectangular format of tidy data. Some really common ones are time series data, which is its whole own thing. And then XML or [[JSON]] data, that stands for extensible markup language or [[JavaScript]] object notation, that's used for hierarchically structured and labeled data, the kind that you frequently get from the web. Or even just compound values, a cell that has two variables in it, so, let me give you an example of each, and how you can use R, to take that untidy data and clean it up and get it in a format
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-tidy-data?u=76281980&t=188)** that's going to be a lot easier to work with for your analyses. Now this is set up in a way that makes sense to humans, but it's not necessarily how it's represented in the data set itself. So for instance, if I ask for just the first few observations, you know, it gives me these numbers without me knowing what year or month they're associated with. On the other hand, we can plot the data just using the generic plot command from R. And here you can see sunspots varying over time in predictable patterns. But let's take the data and put it into a tidy format that matches what we expect data to look like. And I have a rather long command here, now I'm just going to give you a little pep talk here. I did not sit down and write this command all in one go, this took me a while to figure out how to make it all work, again, that's why data prep takes 80% of your time, it's figuring out the commands. It's also why I comment my code very meticulously, and I save my code so I can use it for later examples. But let me show you what's going on here. This says I'm going to create a new object

This can be a very nice way to take what really was an unwieldy data set, and put it in a format that's going to work better for your purposes. And we're going to convert it to a tibble. Then we're going to separate value, that's the name of what's in there, into two columns, first and last, and then we'll print it. Quick and easy. And now you see it down here exactly the way that we would expect to see it. And so, these are several examples of how to take unwieldy data that maybe you wouldn't know how to work with. And by using a combination of functions, in the wrangling process, getting it into a tidy format, where a variable is a column, a row is a case, a cell is a single observation, and that makes it very easy to use the existing R commands with.

And we see from this that the SMI index showed the largest return on investment, or really, the maximum value divided by the minimum value, of the four indices. And that's the general approach to working with the times and dates. Because of the lubridate, we were able to break it down and get our change over time and do a little bit of transformation that is based on this temporal data. Again, it's [[Data Wrangling]]. Do what you need to get the data into the shape to answer the questions that are important to you. And when dates and times are involved,
>
> **[6:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/working-with-dates-and-times?u=76281980&t=373)** the lubridate package, which is included in the tidyverse, is an invaluable tool for doing those tasks.

> [!info]- Semantic Content

### 6. Recoding Data

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Recoding categorical data](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/recoding-categorical-data?u=76281980)
