#### [Recoding quantitative data](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/recoding-quantitative-data?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/recoding-quantitative-data?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] You always want to make sure that data is in the right form for the work that you're doing, and that can involve recoding variables, and in addition to categorical variables, you may have to recode some quantitative or scaled variables. And actually, this is pretty easy to do in R. Let me start by simply loading a couple of packages. We don't need any special functions for this, aside from the tidyverse which is going to give me the pipes, but I'm going to create some data for use in this one. Now let's start with a really easy one. I'm going to simply take the numbers one through seven and save them to an object, x1, and by putting the command in parentheses, it'll also print it in addition to saving it, so here we saved over here, and here you can see one through seven. Now sometimes, when you have questions, you sometimes flip around the order on things to make sure people don't just put the highest answer all the way down, or sometimes you're legitimately asking about a high score indicates more of something, but over here a low score indicates more of something, and you want them to all be in the same direction, so for instance, high indicates more, and so some of them will be reverse coded and you need to flip them around. So we can take this one through seven, and anytime your scale starts with one, what you need to do is subtract the score from one greater than the high end. I have a one to seven scale, the high end is seven, so I take eight and then subtract the score. And when I do that, you can see right here, it flips it around.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/recoding-quantitative-data?u=76281980&t=93)** One becomes a seven, two becomes a six, and so on. If your scale starts at zero, like here I'm going to save zero to six into x2, here's my zero to six here at the bottom, then you just take the maximum value, six, and subtract the scores from that. So I do six minus x2, and there you can see, it's just flips it around, so this gets you data in the same direction. And if you have a bipolar scale that goes from negative, say, three to positive three, then all you need to do is either subtract the scales from zero or multiply times negative one. They'll do the same thing. So here is negative three through zero up to positive three. We can do zero minus those scores, that flips it around, or we can do times negative one, has the same effect. So any one of those will reverse the scales if necessary to get it so all your questions have the higher number indicating more of what you're looking for. Another common thing is to standardize your scores. So you may have a scale that's got a mean of 12 and a standard deviation of 6 1/2, and that may not be very meaningful. So for instance, a lot of procedures work much better, things like cluster analysis, if your data are standardized, they're all on the same scale. A very common method is to make it so that the mean is zero and the standard deviation is one. There's a built-in function in R to do this, it's called scale.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/recoding-quantitative-data?u=76281980&t=186)** We can even get help on it by doing ?scale, and here it goes, and it doesn't give you the options of changing the mean and the standard deviation, but it lets you choose whether you're going to just adjust the mean or just the standard deviation or do both. So I'm going to start by creating two columns of data in a matrix. So the numbers one through 10, we'll put into two columns, and here you see it, we have one through five on the left and six through 10 on the right. So these are like two variables with different means. But what we can do is we can use the scale function on the entire matrix, and I'm putting scale = FALSE, which means we're only going to center it, we're only going to adjust it to deviations from the means, so when I run that, let me [[Zoom]] in on that a little bit, you see, what it's done is it's taken the central value in each, and it tells us that that was a three and an eight, and it converted all the other scores in each column to how far away they are from that mean, so in both cases, we have negative one, negative two, and then one and two. So that's a centered set of data. But you may also want to change the standard deviation to get things so they have about the same amount of spread. Now in this case, they're identical, but you can still do this by simply having scale = TRUE, but that's the default so you don't even have to say it. So all we need to say here is we want to scale the numbers. That will change it so that the mean is zero for each column and the standard deviation is one.
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/recoding-quantitative-data?u=76281980&t=280)** Now, I know it looks a little more complicated here because we're going to fractional values, but it tells you that that's exactly what it did. This is the standard deviation it was working with, and those are the means. And so we now have what are called Z-scores. On the other hand, it's possible that you want to standardize to some other scale. So for instance, I know a few others that are used occasionally. In personality tests like the MMPI, that's the Minnesota Multifacet Personality Inventory, you get your results as T-scores, that's with a capital T, and those have a mean of 50 and a standard deviation of 10. What's nice about those is it means that everybody has a positive two-digit number, which makes it really easy to work with. What you need to do in that case is first scale to get them to a mean of zero and a standard deviation of one, and then you multiply to change the standard deviation, and you do that first, and then you add to change the mean. So in this case, I'm going to take that original dataset, x4, which simply has the numbers one through 10, you can see 'em over here, I'm going to scale them to convert them to Z-scores, and then I'm going to multiply, now, please note, because I'm using dplyr here, I can't just put the asterisk for multiply, I have to put it in backticks, that's above the tilde on the keyboard. So I put `*` and then in parentheses, the number that I'm multiplying it by, 10, and then I do a similar thing for addition, '+' 50, and when I run that command, you can see right here that this in the middle,
>
> **[6:18](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/recoding-quantitative-data?u=76281980&t=378)** that's the mean of 50, and then this'll be a standard deviation of 10. Or there's another scale that's often used. For example, IQ tests are often standardized so that the mean is 100 and the standard deviation is 15. And it's the same procedure. First, standardize it to mean of zero, standard deviation of one, then multiply to get the standard deviation to what you want, then add to move the mean. So we multiply by 15 and then we add by 100, we do that, and now we've gotten our data into several different formats, the original data, just the centered deviations, the Z-scores, the T-scores, these IQ scores, any one of those can be used to get your data into the proper format for the procedures that you're using and for the insight you're trying to get from your data.

#### [Transforming outliers](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/transforming-outliers?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/transforming-outliers?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Every procedure makes assumptions about the data that it's dealing with in order to behave as properly. One of the biggest problems you can have in the vast majority of procedures is outliers, extreme values that tend to exert undue influence on the results of your analyses. And so it's very important to be aware of the existence of outliers, and it's important to know what some of your options are for dealing with them so you can get results that mean what you think they mean. To do this, I'm going to load a few packages, including the data sets package, which has a small data set that's called islands. Let's get a little bit of information on that. Do question mark islands. And it's the area of the world's major land masses. It's the area in thousands of square miles for any land mass that exceeds 10,000 square miles. Now it's a named vector and it has 48 observations in it. Let's take a look at the actual data. I'll just call islands and then I'll [[Zoom]] in on this. And here it is an alphabetical order. We go from Africa with a value of 11,506 and down to the end of the alphabetical list of Victoria at 82. Remember, this is thousands of square miles. Now, we do have some outliers in here. What we're going to do is check the existence of outliers by first doing a histogram. I'm just going to do a basic histogram. And when we zoom in on that, you can see, yeah, most of these, even though they're
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/transforming-outliers?u=76281980&t=94)** the 48 largest land masses, most of them are still down here at this very bottom end. And then we're just kind of creeping just one or two all the way up to here. And so we definitely do not have a normal distribution. We've got some outliers and that could throw off our analysis. Now, one of the best ways to check for outliers is with a box plot because it marks outliers. So I'm going to do a box plot on the same data set and we'll zoom in on that. And the box plot down here shows the range of the middle 50% of scores. And you can see it's really super compressed. This is the highest non-outlying data point. And then we've got all these outliers up here, including one way, way over here. That happens to be Asia, by the way. But we've got an issue with outliers. And so I want to show you a few simple ways of dealing with outliers. Now, it's true that there's many very sophisticated [[Algorithms]] that deal with outliers in their own ways. If you're using a non-parametric approach, [[Decision Trees]] don't get thrown off by outliers usually, [[Neural Networks]] are going to be more flexible. But for standard analyses like scatter plots and means, you're going to want to deal with these. So let's take a look at some of our options. The first one, kind of the most draconian, is just simply cut off the outliers and throw 'em away. This is appropriate as long as you really only care about the non-outlying scores. And as long as you're specific and clear that you did that, and you're focusing only on the major ones.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/transforming-outliers?u=76281980&t=189)** So one option we have is to first see which ones are which and I'm going to sort the land masses in descending value. So let's run that one and zoom in on it. And so we have Asia here at 16,988. Remember that's thousands of square miles. And then Africa and North America, South America, Antarctica, Europe, and Australia. Those are continents, and of course they're going to be huge. And so we could legitimately say, "Well, we don't really want to focus on continents, we want to focus on islands." That is a way of defining your sample that helps you deal with some of these outliers. So all I'm going to do is filter out the continents. I'm going to use the filter command, and I'm going to say simply give me observations where that value is less than a thousand. So that's going to get rid of everything to Australia and it's going to keep just Greenland and below. And when I do that, that makes a lot more sense. Greenland is still very big for an island. And we can then do a histogram. Let's take a look at that. And you see we still have outliers. That's because Greenland over here and we can do a box plot, but it's not quite as pathological as it was previously. And again, it's 'cause we're redefining the group that we're interested in. Another option not used very often is called winsorizing and it's to bring in the outliers. Now, I'm going to demonstrate it with the islands data. It's kind of silly in this case, but you might want to use it for instance, with times on races, time to graduation, or maybe even some [[Financial Data]].
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/transforming-outliers?u=76281980&t=284)** And all you're doing in that case is you're taking the extreme values and you're changing them to give them the highest non-outlying value. So if you're looking at something like time to graduation, you might say, "Well, we're going to go up to eight years. And then anything after eight, we're just going to code as eight." To do that here I'm going to create a new data set with the islands data. That's what we saw previously. And then what I'm going to do is I'm going to use mutate and say, "If the value is greater than 840, then change it to 840." So this is the test. Test that the value is greater than eight 40. If true, replace it with 840. If it's false, meaning it's not greater than 840, then simply keep the current value. And let's take a look at that. Now, it's kind of a funny dataset 'cause now we have a whole bunch of 840s. Again, doesn't make the most sense with this one, but if you do something like time or number of orders, then it might make more sense in that situation. The guiding principle is to always remember what question are you trying to answer and what are you going to do with the results, that can help you determine whether this kind of approach is useful. We can graph the results now, and you see we got this big bump 'cause now we have a bunch of observations at 840. We can also do the box plot and we have several observations stacked on top of each other here at the right. Now, probably a better way of dealing with this is to simply split this into two groups. We might say, "We have continents, we have islands. Why don't we treat them separately?" So let's go back to what we have here. Here are the observations in order. Let's create a new variable called landmass.
>
> **[6:21](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/transforming-outliers?u=76281980&t=381)** And if the value is less than a thousand, call it an island. If it's greater than a thousand, call it a continent. And let's do that and look at the results. I'll zoom in on that. And you can see these ones are labeled as continents. These ones are labeled as islands, which makes sense. You can say these are fundamentally two distinct groups and we can treat them as distinct now, and that allows us to do our graphics separately. So for instance, this case, I can filter just the continent and I can do the box plot. And now we have the area of continent. And you can see that Asia is no longer an outlier here because being compared to just the other continents, we can do a similar thing for the islands. And Greenland is still an outlier, but at least you can see what the box here looks like. The last option I want to discuss, a very common one also is transforming the data. Now, this is doing a linear transformation where you do the same kind of transformation to all the data, not the chop off one like we had with winsorizing. When you have positively skewed data and all of your values are at least one, then a very common choice is to take a logarithm. Now, let's take a quick look at the graphs we saw before. Here's the histogram for islands on the raw data. You can see it's massively skewed. And here is the box plot. Again, it's almost entirely outliers, but let's take the log and I do that by using the function log, L-O-G. Please note, this is the natural logarithm, the one that's base E.
>
> **[7:53](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/transforming-outliers?u=76281980&t=473)** If you want to base 10 log, then you actually have to use log 10 as your function. In other languages, you would use the natural logarithm as LN. So I just want you to be aware that's a distinction with R. In this case, I'm going to take the logarithm and then let's do the histogram. You can see it still has outliers, but they're not, you know, miles away from everything else. And when we do the histogram for the log transform data, there are outliers, but this is something that is conceivably, you can run with it as it is. And so what we've done is we've done this transformation, it brings in the extreme high positive values and it gets it closer to the normal distribution. The assumption that goes behind so many statistical procedures, which makes it easier to get meaningful, interpretable, and actionable results from your data.

#### [Creating scale scores by counting](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-scale-scores-by-counting?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-scale-scores-by-counting?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Data analysis doesn't have to be hard. You don't have to knock yourself out and do something extraordinarily sophisticated. It turns out that simply counting things is a great way of getting insight into what's happening with your data, and I want to show you how to do this in R. I'm going to come down here and I'm going to load a few packages, including rio, which is for importing data because I'm going to bring in a dataset called "StateData.xlsx." And if you go to the files, you'll see it in here, we go to data, and there it is right there, it contains information from [[Google]] Trends as well as a few other sources on state-by-state popularity of search terms. I'm going to save that as a table into df and select just a few variables that I want. Let's [[Zoom]] in on those. You see we have the state_code, and then we have museum, scrapbook, and modernDance. Now, these are indicating the relative popularity of these as Google search terms on a state-by-state basis. A positive score indicates that that state searches more for that term than other states do. A negative number indicates they search for less than other states do. These are actually z-scores with a mean of zero and a standard deviation of one, although they're weighted, so it's slightly more complicated. But what I want to do is start by counting. Let's take a quick look at how to count whether a state is high on any of these three variables. And I'm going to create an index score.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-scale-scores-by-counting?u=76281980&t=93)** I'm going to call it artsCount, and it simply counts if they're high on museum, and by that I mean above one, then we're going to add one to their score on artsCount, otherwise, they get zero. If they are high on scrapbook using the ifelse as my criterion, then we're going to add one, otherwise we will add zero. And if they're high on modernDance, that means again, a z-score above one, then we'll add one, and otherwise they'll get a zero. So it means that states have a maximum score of three on artsCount, otherwise it's zero. It's a pretty high bar, so we would expect a number of zeros. Let me come up here and do this transformation. And now let's look at the data, and we will sort it by the scores on this new variable. And I'll zoom in here. By the way, I did this one here, print(n = Inf), which means I want to see all of the rows, not just the first 10. And so this is one very simple way. If you're looking at something like customer engagement with your organization, simply counting how many times they've been engaged can be an effective way of getting insight into their interactions.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-scale-scores-by-counting?u=76281980&t=187)** Now, let's come down here and get a histogram of the data, I'm simply going to pull it out, and we'll get that, and you can see that we have our three states, with values of two, we have seven states with values of one, and everybody else has the zero. Now, let's look at another way of creating categories. I'm going to use a different function case_when. And what I'm doing is I'm simply creating a yes/no variable, dichotomous, or binary. It's not actually Boolean 'cause I'm not coding it as true and false. But I'm simply trying to indicate whether a state is high on any of these three variables. So they could be high on one, two, three, they'll get a yes. And the way I do that is I create mutate, that means I'm making a new variable. And I'm going to create a variable called likeArts. I use the case_when function, and then I say when museum > 1, and then this vertical line is the pipe that's above the backslash, that's above the Return key on U.S. keyboards. If museum > 1 or scrapbook > 1 or modernDance > 1, then the tilde says, give that case of value of "yes". And then here TRUE ~ "no", that means otherwise, or in any other instance, give them a no, it's the default value.
>
> **[4:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-scale-scores-by-counting?u=76281980&t=279)** And then we're going to select it and print it out in a few different ways. Now, I do want to let you know that if you're interested in creating scale scores, including just counting, there are a couple of packages. I don't want to demonstrate them right now because this function is enough. But psych and Scale, both give you an enormous number of options and more control over how you can count things, how you can average things, how you can create scale and index scores. And they're worth looking into if this is the sort of operation that will help you get insight and answer the questions that you have with your own datasets.

#### [Creating scale scores by averaging](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-scale-scores-by-averaging?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-scale-scores-by-averaging?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] I want to finish our discussion of recoding data by doing another basic procedure which is averaging scores. Anytime you're measuring something, you know that any one measurement has its own idiosyncratic meaning, and it can be a little bit off of what you intend to measure. That's why you want to get several different perspectives, ask several questions, each of which approaches the topic of interest from a different direction. The idea there is that the idiosyncratic variation of each variable will tend to cancel out, and you'll be left with a clearer image of the signal amidst the noise that you're looking for. Now, I want to show you how to do this by first loading a few packages, including rio, because I'm going to bring in the state data set that I've used previously, and I'm going to keep just a few variables. Let's [[Zoom]] in on this one. This gives us the 48 Continental United States along with their [[Google]] search terms for museum, scrapbook, and modern dance. And the numbers indicate the relative popularity of that search term in that state compared to all other states. If it's positive, they search for it more than other states do. If it's negative, they search less. And elsewhere, I showed you how we can count how often they have high values or whether they have a high value on any of these. But I want to show you how to average the three of these. Now, to do this, we're going to use this relatively quick function, and we're going to use mutate.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-scale-scores-by-averaging?u=76281980&t=92)** And then, I'm going to create a new variable called artsCrafts because it's looking museum and scrapbooking, and modern dance, and we're going to use this function rowMeans. And then we actually have to feed it the data again, tell it the variables that we're including and ask it to remove missing values if we have those. Then we'll arrange it in descending values by this new variable. And take a look at the answers where I ask you to print all of the cases, not just the first 10. So, let's come back up here. And then when I zoom in on that, you can see that we have these states now listed in order by this new variable we've created artsCrafts. So, Utah's at the top because while it's below average on museum, it's extremely high on both scrapbook and modern dance. Gives it an average of 2.83. The next highest isn't even above one. And so, you get to see how these things go through until we come down to the bottom where we had Oregon, which curiously was below average on all three of these. But it's a quick way of taking several variables, each of which has its own little source of noise, averaging them, and hopefully canceling out the noise and getting a better picture of what you're looking for. We can get a histogram of those results because we have a new quantitative variable. And you can see it's not too bad that we got an outlier, that's Utah up here. Now, there are some other packages that make scale creation and scale scoring much easier. I don't want to run through them
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-scale-scores-by-averaging?u=76281980&t=186)** because they're their own entire presentations. They are the psych package and the scale package. And if this is something that you use in your own work, you want to look at these packages more carefully. They're going to give you a big boost in functionality in terms of finding the signal in the noise of your data.

### 8. Exploring Data

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Computing frequencies](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-frequencies?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-frequencies?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When you're ready to start looking at your data, you want to start with the baby steps. Do the most fundamental and usually the simplest and easiest parts first as a way of getting started on exploring what's happening in your data. Now, the simplest kind of data is nominal or categorical data, which simply indicates how many observations there are in a category. And the simplest analysis for this is frequencies. And I want to show you a few different ways of doing frequencies in R. To do this, I'm going to start by loading a few packages and then I'm going to come down and I'm going to load a dataset that I have available in the example data. In fact, if we go to exercise files, if you're running this from an [[RStudio]] project, if you open the project, it would be right there and it includes these relative references to make it easy to find things. I go to Data, and I'm going to be using this one right down here, which has [[Google]] search trends information on the 48 Continental United States. I'm going to use the import function from Rio, and I'm going to save it as a tibble. I'm going to select a small number of variables, the categorical, they're actually character ones as they come in raw, and we'll take a quick look at those, and we'll save them to df, which simply means data frame. And then the psychRegion,
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-frequencies?u=76281980&t=92)** which indicates whether they are from a friendly and conventional region, relaxing, creative, and so on, based on a psychology study published a few years ago. Now, the important thing to note here is that all three of these are character variables. That's what the chr means, so we have character variables. It simply indicates that there's text and that they're different from each other. Now, you can do things with character variables. In fact, I'm going to come down here and I'm going to run the summary function. So we're going to take our data frame df, and we're going to run it through summary. Now, when we do that, we get something that's not very helpful. It simply says that it's a character variable and that there are 48 observations or rows within each. So that's not helpful. And in fact, if you want to reiterate that, we can do it for a single variable. I can use select region and run summary. Again, it just says that it's character and there are 48 rows. With a character variable instead of summary, what you're going to want to use is table. Table will create a list of the frequencies like we're looking for, so I'm going to use that one. Now you can see that we have 12 states in the Midwest, nine in the Northeast, 16 in the South, and 11 in the West. So that's probably what you're looking for in terms of frequencies. On the other hand, I want to show you another way of dealing with this. These are with character variables, but frequently when you have these kind of categories, you actually want to turn them into factors 'cause that lets you do a number of other things in R. So what I'm going to do here is I'm going to take the data frame
>
> **[3:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-frequencies?u=76281980&t=185)** and then I'm going to make some changes to it. Now I'm using something here called the compound operator, compound assignment operator, which means I'm both going to read the existing df data frame, and then I'm going to do some work, and then I'm going to overwrite it, so I'm going to replace it with a new data frame. This is equivalent to writing df get df, and then the pipe character, so it's the special pipe. I'm going to use mutate, and then I'm going to take two of these variables, region and psychRegions, and I'm going to save them as factor. Then when we look at the results, let me zoom in on that, you can see it looks the same as what we had before, but now instead of being chr for character, it's fct for factor. That means R is going to treat these differently. So let's come here and let's use summary again. So I'll use summary and we have the same printout. Now, you'll notice it looks a little different. Previously, it was oriented horizontally. Now, it's oriented vertically. And the reason it does that is because this allows you to have several variables next to each other. In fact, I'm going to run summary on the entire data frame. And when I do that, you see that state code, which is still character, it just tells us there's 48 rows. But for region, it gives us the frequency of the four levels in the factor. And for psychRegions, it gives us the frequencies for the three levels in the factor. So this is a great way to get started on seeing what's in your data. Think of it as a follow up to bar charts
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-frequencies?u=76281980&t=280)** that you may have made elsewhere. I usually start with visualizations, then do numbers to get the precision. But in terms of a numerical or a [[Statistical Analysis]], this approach, making frequencies for categorical variables is an excellent first step.

#### [Computing correlations](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-correlations?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-correlations?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Once you've explored your individual variables, it's time to start looking at associations, which is really the exciting part of what's going on. The most common way to do this is with correlations now. While correlation coefficients are generally designed to be used with pairs of quantitative or scaled variables, they're more flexible than that. But let me show you the canonical example. To do this, I'm going to load a few packages, including a new one. Corrplot is one that I use for a visualization of correlation. So let's start by loading these packages, and then I'm going to come down and load the state data about [[Google]] search terms. And I'm going to save just a few of the search terms to be used as examples here. So I'm going to save that as a DataFrame. Let's take a look at the data down here at the bottom. And you can see we have [[Instagram]], [[Facebook]], retweet, so on and so forth through mortgage as the relative popularity of these Google search terms on a state-by-state basis for the 48 continental United States. Now the most common way to look at this is to simply create what's called a correlation matrix. And I use the built-in function cor. So I take my DataFrame and feed it into cor. And when I do that, I get a lot of numbers here. In fact, it's kind of overwhelming how many numbers there are, so I'm going to go straight to another version of this where I'm going to add one extra step and I'm going to round(2).
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-correlations?u=76281980&t=93)** That means give me just two decimal places instead of all these other ones that I have. So when I run that, it's a little easier to read. What you have here are correlation coefficients. These are numbers that range in absolute value from zero to one, they can be positive or negative. So the printed ranges from negative one up to positive one, and they indicate the strength of a linear association or a straight line association between two variables. If it's zero or close to it, it means there's no straight line association. If it's positive one or negative one, it indicates there's a perfect linear association. All the dots fall exactly on a line, and they can be used to predict each other. A positive value indicates that higher values on one variable are associated with higher values on the other variable. A negative value indicates that higher values on one variable go with lower values on the other variable. So you can see, for instance, that we have right here, -0.04. Now that's very close to zero. It means there's really no linear association between the state-by-state popularity of the search term for Facebook and for Instagram. By the way, a correlation matrix is symmetrical along the diagonal. The diagonal is right here, these are the 1.00, that's each variable correlated with itself. And you can see that this -0.04 here, which is Facebook and Instagram, is the same as this one right here, which is Instagram and Facebook. The order doesn't make a difference. Here, we have university and Instagram that's -0.12.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-correlations?u=76281980&t=189)** And if we go a little further down, we'll see the same thing right here. So it flips along the diagonal. And you can see that we got some strong associations. So 0.55 is pretty strong. States that are more likely to search for entrepreneur are also more likely to search for Instagram. You can see a negative one here. States that are more likely to search for scrapbook are less likely to search for retweet. So that's a good way of getting started with a numerical description of the associations between your variables. But an even better way to do this is with a graphic. And to do this, I'm going to create my correlation matrix again, but I'm going to feed it into corrplot and I get to specify a few things. I'm saying that I want just the upper diagonal. I can say remove the diagonal, that's each variable correlated with itself 'cause those tend to overwhelm the chart and they don't mean anything. And then talk about the order and the way that I present the labels. So let's run that one and then [[Zoom]] in on it. And what you see here are that large dark blue circles indicate strong positive associations. So for instance, states that are more likely to search for GDPR, that's the General Data Protection Regulation, the European Union's online [[Privacy]] act, are also more likely to search for volunteering or for museum. Great, and then a large dark red circle indicates a negative association. So for instance, states that have higher relative popularity
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-correlations?u=76281980&t=283)** for searching for Facebook are less likely to search for volunteering or for a mortgage. So this is actually a really nice way to sort through it very quickly as opposed to trying to read numbers individually. Now if you want to get more detail on a single correlation, you can do this with the cor.test function. So I'm going to run just these two, Instagram and privacy, but I do need to let you know I'm using a new pipe here. I'm using what's called the exposition pipe. That's a dollar sign in between parentheses. What this does is it takes a DataFrame, and then it strips away so much of what makes a DataFrame a DataFrame and makes it available as individual vectors, which is what we need here. So once we do that, we can run this command, and then I'll zoom in here. It tells us that it did a Pearson's product-moment correlation coefficient. That's the standard correlation coefficient, usually just called R. The value is right here at 0.15. That's a pretty weak correlation. We can get a hypothesis test. It gets a T-value of 1.0314 with 46 degrees of freedom. The P-value is - 0.3077. Normally, it needs to be less than 0.05 to be considered significant, so this is nowhere close. We also get a confidence interval for the individual correlation coefficient, and it ranges from -0.139 to positive 0.416. So it's not statistically significant,
>
> **[6:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/computing-correlations?u=76281980&t=376)** but this does give us a much more detailed analysis of that one association. And so these are several different options for looking at correlations, and a great first step of looking at the associations between the variables in your data set.

#### [Creating contingency tables](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-contingency-tables?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-contingency-tables?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When your data consists of categories, nominal or sometimes called discrete variables, you usually want to use something different from a correlation coefficient and a contingency table or a cross-tabulation is a very common way of looking at that. I want to show you how to do these in R. Let's start by loading a few packages and then I'm going to load the state data, the [[Google]] search terms dataset. Except this time, we're not going to look at the search terms, we're going to look at the factors, and that's psychRegions and region of the United States. And I'm going to pull those out. The psychRegions have really long labels, friendly and conventional, temperamental and uninhibited, and that actually makes the table hard to read 'cause they're so long. I'm going to use factor recode, that's fct_recode, to give them shorter labels for now. So I simply say factor recode, I say what variable I'm recoding, and then I give the new label and the old label, new label, old label. And then we'll take a look and we'll print it. Let's come here and let's start by creating a contingency table that I'm actually going to save as a new object called ct for contingency table
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-contingency-tables?u=76281980&t=94)** or a cross-tabulation. I do that by taking the data frame that I've saved as df. I'm going to select the two variables, region and psychRegions, both of which are factors. And then I'm going to create a table and print it. So let's do that one. And you see it's just a tiny little table here. It tells us that of the Midwestern states, 11 of them are friendly and one of them is temperamental. Zero fall into the relaxed category. But in the West, three are friendly, eight are relaxed, and zero are temperamental. Northeast, zero friendly, zero relaxed, nine temperamental. Now let's start looking at what else we can do here. These are frequencies. It's simply counting how many states, how many observations fall into each of these combinations. Maybe we want something else. Maybe if we just want the row frequencies, we can use this special function rowSums. Now, this just gets us back to like doing the frequencies, which we could have done on the individual variable, but it's nice to know that it's possible. But let's look at row percentages. Frequently, instead of looking at the actual numbers here, you want to look at the percentages so you can compare across rows that have maybe different absolute frequencies. To do this, I'm going to take our ct, our contingency table. I'm going to use the prop_table function, and then I'm going to give it an argument of one because that means margin number one is for the row percentages. I'm going to round those to two decimal places. And because they're proportions,
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-contingency-tables?u=76281980&t=186)** I'm going to multiply them times 100 to get something that's more like a percentage. And when I read those, you see that I now have Midwest, 92% of the Midwestern states are friendly, 8% are temperamental. Of the Western states, 27% are friendly, 73% are relaxed, and 0% are temperamental. So a lot easier to compare across, especially because we have different numbers of states within each of these regions. I can do a similar thing for columns. Let's start by getting the column sums to get just the frequencies of each column. And you see they're different. We have 24 friendly, 10 relaxed, 14 temperamental. So getting the column percentages might be a good way of analyzing the differences. To do that, I do the exact same thing, except for prop_table, I use two instead of one. That means margin two, which is the column percentages. Let's run that command. And now you see here that it says of the relaxed states, 20% are in the South, 80% are in the West. Of the friendly states, 46% are in the Midwest, 42% are in the South, 12 are in the West, and so on. And then finally, you can get a total percentage where all of these numbers in there collectively add up to 100. For that, you just don't need an argument, you just use prop_table. And now if you add all of these numbers up, it comes to 100%. In addition to this, you can do a chi squared test. This is a null hypothesis test that's often used for tabular data like this.
>
> **[4:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-contingency-tables?u=76281980&t=279)** And you use the chisq.test function, and I'm going to save that into a new object called chi. Now, I can get the default response without doing that, but there's a reason I'm saving it. You can see it's over here. Now it's a list object. Now, if I just ran the chi square test and didn't save it, I would see this what's called the observed results. So let's run that one. And that just gives us the frequencies that we had originally. We can get the expected frequencies, which are used as the basis for the test. We can get the residuals, which is how far off the expected and the observed are. Then we can get the standardized residual. And this is a part of the values that go into calculating the value for chi squared as part of the inferential test when looking at tabular data like these two factors or categorical variables.

#### [Conducting a principal component analysis](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/conducting-a-principal-component-analysis?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/conducting-a-principal-component-analysis?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Correlations are great for looking at how one variable here is connected with one variable here. But sometimes, you want to look at the associations or connections of an entire group of variables. For example, you may have several questions on a survey that all assess more or less this same thing, but you want to be able to average them or you want to be able to see how they group with one another. To do that, you'll want to do something like principal component analysis or factor analysis. Two very closely related procedures. And I want to show you how to run through this in R. To do this, I'm going to come and load a few new packages. One is called GPA rotation. That stands for gradient projection algorithm rotation. And another one is psych. I've used this before, but the psych package, which is for psychometrics and personality research, has some incredibly useful functions for working with these kinds of analyses. So I'm going to load those packages. And then I'm going to come down and load a new data set. It's called b5 now. If you go to the data that I have saved in the exercise files, you'll see b5 and big5. They're related files, and I have a code book that if you click on it, it'll open up right here. And this is personality data. It's about the big five personality factors, which are extroversion, neuroticism, openness to experience,
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/conducting-a-principal-component-analysis?u=76281980&t=95)** conscientiousness, agreeableness. They might be in different orders, also called the five factor model. These are factors that show up very frequently in a lot of different settings when looking at personality. What I have here is a very large data set by psychology standards. It's nearly 20,000 cases, and so it's going to be a good example of the power of R in working with a data set like this. I'm going to start by importing the b5.csv file, and I'm going to save it to df for data frame. And you see it's 18,930 observations of 50 variables. There are 10 variables each for the five different personality factors, and we're going to see how these things can get combined. But first, let's take a look at the column names, just so you can see a little bit what the data looks like. And here, there abbreviated E1, E2 and so on for extroversion. And then N is for neuroticism, A is for agreeableness, C is for conscientiousness, and O is for openness to experience. Now, when you're doing a principal component analysis, which is sort of like trying to draw a line through a multidimensional space, that adequately summarizes a lot of the variability. So for instance, you look at something like height and weight, they're associated, and you can talk about a principal component which maybe is just size or bigness, and that you can use that instead
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/conducting-a-principal-component-analysis?u=76281980&t=187)** of these two correlated dimensions. It gives you a little bit less that you have to deal with. It maybe cancels out some of the noise, but gets to the essential details for your particular analysis. There are several different functions available for doing principal component analysis and R. The most common is this one right here, prcomp. Another one that's sometimes used that is a legacy from the precursor to R, which was a programming language called S, is printcomp. I actually like to use this one called principal, which is from the psych package. The other two are in R by default. What I'm going to do is I'm going to take our data, df, which has only the outcome questions. I'm going to use the prcomp function to demonstrate the most common method. And I'm going to do two things to the data. Although strictly speaking, they're not necessary for this particular data set. I'm going to center them, which is going to give all of them the same mean of zero. And then I'm also going to scale them, which gives them all the same variance and standard deviation of one. The reason this is important is that if your variables are on different scales, the ones that have a larger scale kind of dominate the analysis and you don't want that. So I'm going to do this analysis just from prcomp. I'm going to center and scale them, and I'm going to save it into an object called pc for principal component. Now, that actually ran really fast
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/conducting-a-principal-component-analysis?u=76281980&t=281)** for what's a relatively large data set, but let's get the summary [[Statistics]] for pc. And I'll have to [[Zoom]] in because that gives us a lot of information. If you're familiar with the theory of principal component analysis, and it's not my purpose here to talk about all of that, then the importance of components here might mean something to you where we have many different components to go up to the 50 different variables we have. And this is a different way of apportioning the variability that's in the data. I'm going to zoom back out. We'll also get a screen plot. And a screen plot has to do with the rubble that falls off the side of a cliff. I'll zoom in on that where the biggest piles are right next to the cliff, and then they taper down in descending order. Now, this one's not labeled, but what it tells you is how much variability or how much variance each component accounts for. And this first one accounts for eight units of variance. That's an eigenvalue. The next one accounts for four and so on and so forth. We get to five here, and this lets us know that even though we have 50 variables in our data, we might be able to boil it down a smaller number. And a screen plot here is one of the common tools for assessing how many components you should have in your data. Now, I happen to know that this one's designed to have five components, and so the fact that we've got this little jump here after five makes sense to me.
>
> **[6:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/conducting-a-principal-component-analysis?u=76281980&t=376)** But if you're trying to figure out how many factors or components you should have in your data, there are a few other approaches you can use. One that you may not be familiar with is something called very simple structure or VSS. The idea here is that when you do principal components analysis, it actually gives you these weights that you can use to multiply every variable buy to get a new component score for everything. But in practice, people usually put a variable onto only one component. They average a degree, just one score, and very simple structures and attempt to find how many components you need when you're going to put a variable into only one component. To do this, I'm going to simply run the data frame. I'm going to select my first 50 variables, which are all the ones that are in there anyhow. And then I'm going to do VSS, and I'm going to tell it to run up to 10 possible factors or components, but you don't need to show it all to us. Now, when I run that, I get this graph off to the side, which deserves a little bit of explanation. This is telling me how well the model fits the data. It goes from zero down here, which is horrible, up to one, which is perfect. We don't expect it to get to one. And this is telling us how many factors it's adding. You can see it goes one, two, three, four, five, six, up to 10. What these numbers and the lines indicate is how many components are we going to allow each variable to contribute to? Well, in practice, it should be just one.
>
> **[7:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/conducting-a-principal-component-analysis?u=76281980&t=470)** So we'll even ignore those other ones. But here you can see that we have one factor, two, three, four, five. And at five, it flattens out and even goes downhill a little bit. This is an indication that maybe a five factor model is appropriate, which makes sense 'cause that's what it was designed for. I'm going to do another thing here. The VSS also includes an N factors, which gives us a few other looks at this question. All I need to do is change this last line to say nfactors, and again, I'll say a maximum of 10. I'll run that one and zoom in on it. And this top left one is a repeat of the graph that we just looked at, but we also have three other graphs, the complexity, the empirical BIC for Bayesian Information Criterion and the root mean residual. And you'll see that each of these shows something distinctive going on at five factors. Dips down. Here, we're flattening out at five here, we got to bend right at five. Taking together all of these, suggest that five factors would be appropriate solution for our data. And then from there, I'm going to actually do a factor analysis. This is closely related to a principal component analysis. They have a different theory about the relationship between the factors and the variables, but they're often used interchangeably. To do this, I'm going to calculate factors using the fa function. So I'm going to select my data, get the variables I want, use the fa function, tell it that I want five factors.
>
> **[9:25](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/conducting-a-principal-component-analysis?u=76281980&t=565)** This is based on my very simple structure analysis. We're going to use the oblimin oblique rotation. It's a way of simplifying the interpretation of the data. We'll get a diagram and we'll print the results. By the way, notice I'm using the T pipe here, which is a way of feeding all these results to both this and this without stopping in between. So I'm going to run this command, and let's take a look at the output here on the bottom. I'm going to zoom up on that. And we've got a substantial amount of information here because it's giving us the 50 variables and the five factors along with things like the communality and the uniqueness. And again, my point here is not to tell you about the theory of factor analysis, but to show you how you can get the information you need to interpret your results. We've got a fair amount of information there. Let me zoom back out and then look at my chart here. And what this is telling us, even though it's kind of microscopic, are five different factors, which is just calling MR1 and so on because that's how I'm rotating things. And then these are the individual variables. Now, the black line to indicate positive connections, the dotted red indicate negative, but potentially still meaningful associations. The nice thing is this all falls into the categories we expect very easily, very quickly. And then I'm going to do just a little bit more. I'm going to do hierarchical clustering. Now, I've done this before with the clustering of cases
>
> **[10:59](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/conducting-a-principal-component-analysis?u=76281980&t=659)** that is groups of people or units of observation. This time, I'm going to do with variables. There's a different approach, but all I need to do is call the iclust function. And when I do that, it gives us a fair amount of output there but it's this graph on the right that's most interesting. I'll zoom in on it. And again, if you have a larger screen, what it lets you do is look at the 50 different variables here and at each step how they get combined with one another. And what you'll find is that these fall into relatively natural clusters for what the 50 questions go into the five personality factors. And if we want to, we can do a principal component with a set number of factors, since I know that five is a good solution based on our other things. I'm going to do that right here. I'm going to use principal that is the principal component method that's in the psych package. And I specify five factors, and we get this output right down here, which is actually very similar to some other output that we've seen. And then I'm going to run it again with an oblique rotation and get a plot of the position of the variables on each of the components. So I'm going to run that one. And when we zoom in on the plot on the right, these are the five components. Curiously, they're not listed one through five in order. But these give us an indication of how much each of the variables load on the different factors that we have.
>
> **[12:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/conducting-a-principal-component-analysis?u=76281980&t=754)** And so it is again, another way of looking at the associations of individual variables and how they might be combined into larger factors or components, which both reduces the number of things we have to deal with. And it can help cancel out some of the idiosyncratic variation of individual variables to give a clearer lock on the signal in the noise in your data.

When you have a one to five scale and you want to flip around the answers for some of the questions, all you need to do is subtract a person's answer from one more than the highest value. So if it's a one to five scale, do six minus the score on that question. If it's a one to seven, subtract it from eight. If it's a zero to something scale like zero to 10, you simply subtract their score from the highest score. And if it's a negative to positive, like negative three to positive three or negative 10 to positive 10, then you can either multiply it times negative one or you can subtract their answer from zero. Either one will accomplish the same thing. And so I've got some other functions here that if you wanted to, you could un-comment them and use them. The dataset that I have has answers on one to five scales. #### [Conducting a confirmatory factor analysis](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/conducting-a-confirmatory-factor-analysis?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/conducting-a-confirmatory-factor-analysis?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] I want to finish our discussion of preparing data for analysis by giving you a very quick overview of a sophisticated and important procedure, confirmatory factor analysis or CFA. To do this, I'm going to be using a new package, and that is called Lavaan, which is a portmanteau for latent variable analysis, and it's the probably most common package in R for doing both confirmatory factor analysis and structural equation modeling or SCM. Let me load these packages here, and then I'm going to come down, and I'm going to use the same B5 data set. This is the big five personality factors and what we have there are 50 different variables that are designed to fall onto five groups or factors that measure these large scale personality attributes: extroversion, openness to experience, agreeableness and so on. I'm going to save that data to df, and then we're going to just check the variable names. Now, in some analyses, it's important to get all of your variables pointed in the same direction, so we would have to reverse some. That's something I did in item analysis, but I don't need to do that right here. It works just fine with the variables going both positive and negative as long as they still have a good absolute
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/conducting-a-confirmatory-factor-analysis?u=76281980&t=94)** value for their association with the factor. What I have to do for Lavaan to work properly is I have to define the factors, and so I'm going to create a new object called cfa.model And then in quotes, I'm going to do this. I'm going say, the first factor or the first component is going to be called extra V for extroversion, and then I have an equal sign and a TILDA that lets it know that I'm writing a formula and then I'm giving the variable names, E1 plus E2 plus E3, and it actually likes you to spell them out manually. I'm sure there are other ways to do it, but I was able to just copy and paste and do that for neuroticism, agreeableness, conscientiousness, and openness. And so it's a long statement, but it allows me to specify with great precision what variables go into which factors. And so I'm going to save that and you can see it's just this values here. And then I'm going to take that information and I'm going to apply that description, the CFA model to my data, which is DF using the CFA function, and I'm going to save it into an object called fit. Now, this can get rather complicated. I've got almost 20,000 observations, 50 variables isn't a whole lot. But this can take a few minutes depending on your computer. And you see here we've got the fit saved now, and it's a large Lavaan object at 16 and a half megabytes,
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/conducting-a-confirmatory-factor-analysis?u=76281980&t=189)** which is, I mean, there's a lot of stuff there because that's bigger than the data set. We're going to do just one tiny little thing here. I'm going to ask it to summarize the fit, we're going to look at the summary of the model fit, and we're going to ask for fit measures. Now, this gets you a lot of output, and when I run this, I'm going to [[Zoom]] in on that and I'm going to scroll back up to the top of that command. All right, there we go. Again, confirmatory factor analysis is a nuanced, subtle, profound approach, and there's a lot of work that goes into this. I'm just trying to give you the tiniest little example to let you know that R can do it, that Lavaan is the package you want to use, and this is what it looks like when you get your output. Probably the part that we're most interested in is this right here, user model versus baseline model. And we have both the comparative fit index and the Tucker-Lewis index. We have two numbers here, which are 0.735 and 0.748. Now, in an ideal world, these numbers are going to be higher than that. They're going to be 0.9 or maybe even 0.95 as a way of indicating a very precise fit between our theoretical model of the factors and the observed data. So these numbers are a little low. It lets us know that this is not a perfect fit, but there are a lot of things that can influence this. And again, I actually take a pragmatic approach to a lot of psychometrics.
>
> **[4:44](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/conducting-a-confirmatory-factor-analysis?u=76281980&t=284)** You can use the number to help inform your decision making, but the numbers don't make the decisions for you. You want to use your own understanding of what's going on, your knowledge of the individual questions, the people who answered them, and see what kind of answers make sense to you. Does it make sense to combine these questions? Does it make sense to interpret them in a particular way? And a CFA confirmatory factor analysis can be one very important piece of information in helping you make those judgements and keeping you honest in the process. But this combined along with our item analysis, our principle component analysis, or our factor analysis, all of those can be different ways of getting perspectives on your data. Again, with the goal of isolating the signal in the noise, canceling out some of the idiosyncratic variants of individual variables, and focusing on the concepts and the components and the factors that are of the greatest utility to you and the greatest informativeness for answering the questions that you are asking of your data.

>

That's the true mean greater than 4.6. And we have a confidence interval that because it's directional it's one-sided. It's as low as 4.599 and as high as infinity, so we just barely dip below that. #### [Comparing paired means: Paired samples t-test](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/comparing-paired-means-paired-samples-t-test?u=76281980)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/comparing-paired-means-paired-samples-t-test?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When you're trying to produce an effect in the real world, you have an intervention of some kind. It might involve creating an ad campaign for a company. It might involve having a special curriculum for a class. It might involve having a patient go on a particular medical regimen. Any one of these is designed to produce a change from how things were at the beginning to how things were later on. A very common test in this situation is a paired samples t-test that looks at data for two paired samples, usually the same people, at time one and time two. Let me show you how to do this in R. > **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/comparing-paired-means-paired-samples-t-test?u=76281980&t=280)** represents the distribution of sales of the 30 items after the campaign. Probably the most important one though is what's called a parallel coordinates plot, and this is a line diagram, and this is why I had to load the other package, GGally. I'm telling it splineFactor is equal to true, that actually spreads out the categories a little bit, and we're going to have a little bit of transparency in the lines. So let's make that one here. And these are sometimes called spaghetti charts, because they kind of look like a mess if you have a lot of data. We only have 30 data points, but these are the sales for each item at time one, and this line connects it to its corresponding value at time two. The question is whether the lines are mostly going uphill or whether they're going downhill? I do see a few downhill lines. You got one right here that's going downhill. A lot of them seem flat. But this is a way of looking at the time one, time two. Now you could also do a scatter plot,
>
> **[6:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/comparing-paired-means-paired-samples-t-test?u=76281980&t=373)** and if you are able to draw a reference line at exactly 45 degrees, you can get the same general idea, but this is a nice way of visualizing the change. Now the actual inferential test that we're going to use is the t-test. And the thing with the paired t-test is it does subtraction, it takes one number, it subtracts the other, and then it does all the analysis on that difference. The important thing is to remember which one you need to put in first when you do the command. Although it's counterintuitive, you put time two first, you put the outcome first, that's the minuend, that's the number subtracted from. And the reason is that way, if scores go up over time, you'll get a positive difference. And so the time one score, the subtrahend, that's the number that is subtracted, is listed second. So time two minus time one gives us the difference we're interested in. The really important thing here is this, that we tell it that we're doing a paired t-test. And when we run that command, here's our information down at the bottom, it says, t2 and t1, that we have a T-score of 13.69, we have 29 degrees of freedom, and we have a P value that's really, really low. We had to go to scientific notation, it's practically zero. The null hypothesis is a difference of zero. The 95% confidence interval for the difference from time one to time two goes from 10.26 to 13.86. And the mean difference is 12.066,
>
> **[7:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/comparing-paired-means-paired-samples-t-test?u=76281980&t=469)** which is kind of funny because I set it up to be on average around 10, so randomization produces funny things. But this lets us know that there was a reliable difference. It's an average of 12. It's absolutely statistically significant. Now we do have a few options with the paired t-test. We can do all this stuff that's the same at the beginning. By the way, in case you didn't notice, I'm using the exposition pipe up here, that's with the dollar sign in the middle as opposed to the greater than sign, because the t-test wants vectors and not a data frame. So I do df, the exposition pipe t.test. I set time two, time one is paired. I can specify a population mean for the basis of all this. And I say, "Is the difference greater than 10?" I can also say that I want a directional hypothesis. It can be greater than, less than, I'm going to use greater. And can change the confidence level for the confidence interval, let's change it to 80%. When I run that command, we get the same layout for the output here. Now you can see, however, that our value of T is 2.3448, because we're comparing it to a population value of 10. And our P value is 0.01305, so it's still statistically significant. It's comparing it to a population value of 10. The actual observed difference is 12.066. And our confidence interval ranges from a low of 11.31 to infinite because it's a directional hypothesis.
>
> **[9:28](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/comparing-paired-means-paired-samples-t-test?u=76281980&t=568)** And so, again, if you're looking at the effect of an intervention of a program of a campaign, then a paired samples t-test is usually going to be your first choice, and R gives you a number of options for setting that up and for visualizing the information. And so you can get the insight you need to assess the effectiveness of the intervention or the program using a pair samples t-test.

> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/comparing-means-with-multiple-categorical-predictors-factorial-analysis-of-variance?u=76281980&t=280)** a factorial analysis of variance is you start with your outcome variable. In this case, it's breaks. Then you use a tilde to say, as a function of or predicted by, the kind of wool plus the tension of the wool, plus the interaction of the two. And you can specify that either with an asterisk in between the two or with a colon. And then data. That simply means that the data argument goes from DF and it goes to this placeholder for the dot. And I'm going to save that in a generic model object called fit. That's a common name. So we run that. We don't get any output, but you can see that we've got a new thing over here in the environment. Let's get a summary of the fit. And this is the analysis of variance table that you're normally looking for. We have result for wool up here. The wool in and of itself doesn't make much of a difference. We have one degree of freedom 'cause there's two wools. We have the sum squares, the mean squares. The value for F is 3.765, almost significant. And that tells us that you know this one, if you were to average these three, it's a little bit higher than these three over here, but doesn't reach standard levels of statistical significance. On the other hand, tension makes a big difference. If you were to average of the two red ones, that's different than the average of the two green ones, and that's different than the average of the two blue ones. That's the highly significant finding with a P value of 0.000693.
>
> **[6:15](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/comparing-means-with-multiple-categorical-predictors-factorial-analysis-of-variance?u=76281980&t=375)** The three asterisk indicate that that's less than the standard 0.001 level of significance. And then the interaction, it has an F value of 4.189 and a P-value, [[Probability]] value of 021044. And so that is also statistically significant. It's not as big as the effect of tension overall, but it is a qualifier of tension. Now you can get additional information on each of these models. You can get the models table, which is the table of effects, or how much you should add for each column. Let me zoom in on that one. It says add 2.889 for wool A, subtract the same amount for B. These are contrasts. And then we have the interaction contrasts in here. That's a little hard to read. Most of the time what you're actually looking for is just the table of means. And for that, I use the same model.tables, except this time I add the argument type equals means, and that gives me the marginal means. And so we've got the overall means, the marginal means for wool, for the tension, and then for the six different cells. Finally, you may want to do a post hoc test to see exactly where the differences come up. Now, in a three by two, this gets a little complicated. I'm going to use the TukeyHSD, which is for [[John the Ripper|John]] Tukey.
>
> **[7:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/comparing-means-with-multiple-categorical-predictors-factorial-analysis-of-variance?u=76281980&t=470)** Honestly, significant difference tests, and we're going to get a lot of printout on this one. These are all the different comparisons that it does, and we're looking for an adjusted P value, probability value less than 0.05. This one's close, this one makes it, this one makes it. And then you get all these different comparisons of the cells. There's a lot going on there. You don't really want to be just fishing around, but try to see something that makes sense to you that's interpretable, fits with your understanding of the phenomenon at hand. But when you do this, it's a great adjunct to the original analysis in terms of giving you insight into your data and helping you explain how the results depend on the combination of this factor and this factor. And hopefully that gives you some actionable insight into solving problems and doing something useful with your data.
