#### [Predicting outcomes with quantile regression](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/predicting-outcomes-with-quantile-regression?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/predicting-outcomes-with-quantile-regression?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] One of the worst things you can have in data analysis is an outlier. And when you're doing a univariate analysis and looking at things like the mean, you know that a single outlier can arbitrarily distort or destroy the mean so that it doesn't tell you anything close to what you want. When it comes to associations like regression, outliers can be even worse depending on the nature of what's going on. And methods like least squares [[Linear Regression]], that's the standard regression can get thrown off hugely. Fortunately, there are ways to deal with this with robust methods. Robust means not as easily influenced by outliers, and one of the best ways is with quantile regression. Think of that as an analogy to the median as opposed to the mean, but applied to the association of variables and regression. To show you how this works, I'm going to load a few packages including one called quantreg for quantile regression, and then I'm going to load the state data that we've seen before, which gives us state by state relative popularity of search terms on [[Google]]. And we've seen this several different times, but here are the data and we'll get a scatter plot of all of them with ggpairs. Again, this can take a minute, and let's [[Zoom]] in on that. Now, we've looked at this before, but what you see here, we have some variables with outliers. [[Privacy]] has both low and high outliers,
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/predicting-outcomes-with-quantile-regression?u=76281980&t=95)** but a really interesting one is down here, scrapbook has a high outlier and so does modern dance, and in fact, that's a high bi varied outlier. It's going to be a lot easier to see if we focus on that individually. So let's come over here and make a scatterplot of just scrapbook and modern dance. And what you see here is a whole bunch of points. 47 of the states have this pattern over here, and then we've got one that's a massive outlier on both the two variables independently, and it has a very unusual association between the two. Now, I happen to know that this is the state of Utah where I live, and we are very, very high on Google searches for both scrapbook and modern dance. And the association between them seems to be very different from the [[Representational State Transfer (REST)|rest]] of the United States. So I'm going to show you what the effect of this is. One is I'm going to do this scatterplot again, but this time to put at least squares linear regression line on it. And when I do that, you can see what happens when you have a bi-variate outlier like Utah is here. It pulls that line up in its direction, it exerts extraordinary amounts of influence for a single data point. And so this is a problem 'cause you can tell that is not the right line. It doesn't fit the data in general, it's just the influence of that bi-variate outlier is throwing it off. And so let me show you a different method. We can add a median regression line to do that.
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/predicting-outcomes-with-quantile-regression?u=76281980&t=188)** I'm going to run the same command, but this time the ggplot command geom quantile, that lets you put in a quantile regression line and you can add any quantile you want between 0 and 100. And you can put in several, I'm going to use just the 50th percentile or 0.5 quantile, which is the median, and I'll make it red and show you what it looks like then. And now when we zoom in on that, the red line seems to do a much better job of capturing the relationship between these two variables and basically ignoring this massive outlier here. You can see, in fact, the blue line has a positive slope and the red line has a negative slope. Again, because we're focusing on quantiles as opposed to the mean, we're able to capture that relationship and ignore the outlier. But I want to show you not just how to do this graphically, but how to do this numerically, how to actually compute a regression for that red line. To do that, I'm going to use the rq, think of that as for regression quantile command from quant reg. And to do it, I say this is my outcome variable, modern dance. And then tilde means as predicted by or as a function of scrapbook. Data, the dot here simply means go back to this thing that came before the pipe. Tau that tells what quantile to use. And in this case I'm saying use 0.5, the median, and then print the results of that and save the model into the global environment. So we do that.
>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/predicting-outcomes-with-quantile-regression?u=76281980&t=282)** You can see it's over here now. And here we have the intercept and the slope for the red quantile regression line. If you want the actual coefficients, we can do summary. Now again, because this is done through an iterative process, we get a lower bound and an upper bound. And it may not be unique, but this gives us an idea of what's going on. And then we can actually do T test using a bootstrap standard error. And when we run that, you see that we have tau that tells us what quantile are we using, we're using the 0.5, and we have the value of the intercept and the slope of the line, the standard error, the T value, and then the [[Probability]] value or P value of that T value. And what you can see here is that this one right here is statistically significant, and that lets us know that the negative slope of the red line is something that is probably reliable and probably accurately reflects a negative slope in the population overall for the quantile regression. And so this is a very simple model, but it's a dramatic demonstration of the effect of outliers, especially bi variant outliers on a method like regression. And how using a quantile based approach, in this case, the median can be more effective and better reflect the obvious pattern in the data than the standard least squares linear model. It's a better way of finding the meaning in the data
>
> **[6:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/predicting-outcomes-with-quantile-regression?u=76281980&t=376)** that reflects the intuitive perception of it, and that's going to help you make better decisions that reflect the reality of what's happening in your data.

#### [Predicting outcomes with logistic regression](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/predicting-outcomes-with-logistic-regression?u=76281980)

#### [Predicting outcomes with Poisson or log-linear regression](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/predicting-outcomes-with-poisson-or-log-linear-regression?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/predicting-outcomes-with-poisson-or-log-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Standard models of regression are designed for continuous outcomes that measure on a scale of some kind, like an average. But when what you are trying to model is frequencies or how many cases fall into a category, you need to use a different model. And the most common approach goes by two different names. It's either called Poisson regression or log-linear models. They're the same thing. And I want to demonstrate this by first loading up R packages. And then I'm going to come down and use an example data set that's in R. It's called InsectSprays. Let's get a little bit of information about that one. And what I like about this is that it's historical. It's from a paper published in 1942. But it's about six different kinds of insect sprays and how many bugs they each killed. That's what's called count. Let's save that data. InsectSprays to df, for data frame. And let's take a quick look at the first few rows. And what you have here are counts of how many bugs were killed, zero, one, up to whatever. And you have the different sprays. We can summarize the data with summary. Now let's take a look at the effectiveness of each of these sprays. We'll use box plots to do that. I'm going to take the data frame, and I'm going to feed it into ggplot, and see that the x,
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/predicting-outcomes-with-poisson-or-log-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=93)** the thing that we're breaking it down by, is the spray, and y, the quantity that we're depicting, is the count of the number of bugs killed. And then we'll also make it different colors for the sprays. And when we do that, we get this box plot. Let me [[Zoom]] in on that. And what you have are the six different sprays, A, B, C, on up to F. And this is the number of bugs killed. And you can tell by this that three of them, A, B, and F, are very different from the others, C, D, and E. But we can model that with a Poisson regression. Now very quickly, Poisson regression, which literally is French for fish. But it actually comes from a person's name, the French mathematician, Simeon Denis Poisson. And it's also called a log-linear model. And to do this, we are going to use GLM. That is, R is built in generalized linear model function. And we say that we want to model the count of the number of bugs killed, and then tilda means as a function of or as predicted by the spray. And you say family = 'poisson'. That lets it know that we're doing a Poisson regression. data = ., that says just use this item from above, the pipe, and then we'll get a summary of it. So let's do those. And when we do that, we get this output. It gives us a little bit of information about the residuals, and then the coefficients. And it gives a separate coefficient for each of the sprays minus 1. You can't have all of them with their own coefficient.
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/predicting-outcomes-with-poisson-or-log-linear-regression?u=76281980&t=186)** 'cause then you have what's called collinearity. So it takes spray A as the basis, and then gives a separate coefficient for each of them, B, C, D, E, and F. And you can see that three of them are significantly different, C, D and E. Those were the ones that were much lower, which is why they have negative coefficients. And then you have information about the overall effectiveness of the model. But really what this shows you is that Poisson regression for count or frequency data is really easy to set up in R, and, along with a graphic that shows you the information, is really easy to interpret.

#### [Assessing predictions with blocked-entry models](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/assessing-predictions-with-blocked-entry-models?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/assessing-predictions-with-blocked-entry-models?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Sometimes you're trying to find a little bit of simplicity, a little bit of order within your data, and you're trying to find the simplest model that you can use to predict a particular outcome. Or you may be focused on what unique contribution do a certain set of your variables make. Now, there are many different ways to go about this. You can do what's called a stepwise regression, which looks at the variable with the strongest bivariate association with your outcome as that, then removes as influence and gets partial correlations for the others and picks the one with the highest and so on and so forth. That tends to capitalize on chance and it gets the quirks of the data. There are other approaches, lasso regression, ridge regression, stagewise regression, lots of others, but I actually really prefer a method that I call blocked regression. It's sometimes called nested models, and you can also think of it as hierarchical models, but those are also used to refer to multi-level models where you have information at different levels of observation, like students within the classroom and then classrooms within the school, then schools within the district. But I want to show you what I really like about the blocked regression approach because it allows you to use theory to decide what should go in and then empirically assess the unique contribution of each set of variables. Now to do this, I'm going to actually be relying on some code from a different application called jamovi. And jamovi has a package that runs in R called jmv. So I'm going to load jmv as well as some other packages.

#### [Grouping cases with hierarchical clustering](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/grouping-cases-with-hierarchical-clustering?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/grouping-cases-with-hierarchical-clustering?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Marketing and medicine and so many other applied domains. One of the most important questions is what cases, what people, what organizations go with whom? If you're in marketing, you may want to send out the same appeals to similar groups of people. If you're in medicine, you want to find out which patients are similar to one another so you can make a better prognosis and treatment plan. Underlying both of these is cluster analysis, the idea that you want to take the data that you have, and based on that data, find out which cases are most similar to one another. Now, the important thing about this is there's judgment calls in this one, because similar is a psychological concept, it's a construct, and there are many different ways of going about that. I want to show you one of the most flexible is called hierarchical clustering. Now, to do this, I'm going to load a few packages including one called cluster, another called factoextra. We can do cluster analysis, but these give us some extra options in how we go about it. Then I'm going to load the state data. This is state by state popularity of [[Google]] search terms from a few years ago. Now what I'm going to do is I'm going to find out which states are similar to each other based on these search patterns, and that's an enormous qualification. I can only cluster them based on the data that I have. And if I'm only feeding in two variables, it's only going to be able to compare them on those two. I'm feeding in a small number. 13 variables, including the state code.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/grouping-cases-with-hierarchical-clustering?u=76281980&t=94)** So this is by no means comprehensive, but if you're doing a marketing campaign, you really only care about how people are similar. Who are your clients? Who are your potential customers? And so let's take a look at how we can do this with hierarchical clustering. Now, the first thing we need to do is we need to compute the clusters. And we're going to be using two functions. I'm going to take the data that I've saved as df, I'm going to compute the distance or the dissimilarity between the cases how far apart are they in a multidimensional space? And then I'm going to use the function hclust, which computes hierarchical clustering. Now, I do want to say one thing that's really important. Your data need to be on similar scales because if you have one variable that's zero out of a million and one that's zero to one, the one that's out of a million is going to completely dominate the algorithm. These data, these Google search terms are all Z scores. They have a mean of zero, a weighted mean of zero, and a standard deviation of about one. So they're already scaled. If your data are not scaled, you simply need to run the scale function to do that first. But let's take this and compute hc for hierarchical clusters. Now I'm saving this into a new object 'cause there's a few things I'm going to do with it. Most importantly, I'm going to plot it and I actually can use the generic XY plotting. And so here at the bottom we have all of the states listed. There's Connecticut, there's Rhode Island, there's California. And when states are close to each other like these two, that's South Carolina and Tennessee, it says based on the data that I gave it, which was 12 Google search terms those two states are relatively similar. when states don't come together until very high up in the chart, that means they're very different from the other ones. That, for example, is Utah. And again, based on the data that I gave it. Now, the reason this is called hierarchical is because at the top they're all together, at the bottom they're all separate. And it splits it at each step as we go through. And you can see different categories at each step as we go through. The hierarchical clustering algorithm using actually is called agglomerative, which means they all start separately and they get joined together. But because it goes in this step by step process, it's a hierarchical method. Now let's [[Zoom]] back out. One big question is how many clusters do you actually want? Because one cluster's no good, there's no point in doing that.
>
> **[4:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/grouping-cases-with-hierarchical-clustering?u=76281980&t=280)** And having a number of clusters equal to the number of cases, again, accomplishes nothing. Your goal is to simplify it for your purposes. For a marketing campaign, maybe you just want two or three, 'cause that's all the time you really have to develop different campaigns. If it's medicine, on the other hand, you may need to be more micro and have more clusters. But there are a few guidelines for choosing how many clusters, aside from the obvious, how much time and money do I have for dealing with different clusters? There are some graphics you can use to help you choose how many clusters you should have. One here we simply call it the elbow method. It involves something called within cluster sum of squares, which has to do with the distance in a multidimensional space. Now I'm going to graph this and because I've looked at it before, I'm going to draw a reference line at five. So let's run this one. And what we're looking for is a bend in the curve. Problem is it's kind of a smooth curve, so there's no super obvious bend, but you know not really at five, maybe at two, maybe at six. But I'm going to draw one at five just because. Another method is the silhouette method. Now if you imagine taking a flashlight and shining it on your hand, you can see a shadow on the wall. If you hold your hand parallel to the wall, you see the whole silhouette, you can tell that it's a hand. But if you rotate your hand 90 degrees, you have no idea what it is. You can reduce the dimension going from a three dimensional object

> **[6:11](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/grouping-cases-with-hierarchical-clustering?u=76281980&t=371)** to a two dimensional silhouette and still maintain most of the information if you have it at the right angle. And the silhouette method is a way of telling us how many dimensions we need to still have recognizable data. So let's do this one. And we're looking for something called the maximum width. And when we do that, let's zoom in on it. This is the average silhouette width, and here it maximizes at two clusters and then it drops down pretty quickly. So this one says two, maybe three might be ideal, would be sufficient. And then there's the third method, the gap method. And this involves what's called the total intra cluster variation for a collection of values of k. K is how many groups there are, and it compares them to the expected values from a null distribution, which it gets from doing Monte Carlo simulations. So let's do this one. We have to remove the non-numeric variable that's the state code. And we're simply going to look for the highest value when we do this. So let's get that graph it takes a moment because it's doing a lot of repeated calculations, and here it actually says one. #### [Grouping cases with k-means clustering](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/grouping-cases-with-k-means-clustering?u=76281980)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/grouping-cases-with-k-means-clustering?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When you're looking for clusters in your data, the cases, or the people that are similar to each other, sometimes you actually know how many groups there should be, how many you already have various campaigns prepared for, or how many categories you can accommodate. In that case, you don't want to use a hierarchical method, which goes all the way from one giant group to everybody in their own group. But you want to use something like [[k-means clustering]]. And what this means is you select a number, that's the k that indicates a previously chosen number of clusters that you want. This is a really easy to do approach in R and I'm going to run through this by first loading a few packages including cluster. #### [Classifying cases with k-nearest neighbors](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/classifying-cases-with-k-nearest-neighbors?u=76281980)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/classifying-cases-with-k-nearest-neighbors?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When you're trying to decide how to classify a case, is this a yes or a no? Is it junk mail? Is it legitimate mail? Or what kind of talents does this person have? The best way to do it is to see what the other ones that are similar to it have. This is the idea behind K-nearest neighbors, and the way it works is, you find a data point that needs to be classified, and then you see where it is located in a multidimensional space where each dimension is variable and then you simply look at the ones that are closest to it. Maybe you look at three or five or 29 that are closest to it, and then you use the categorization that is common among those. Or if it's a quantitative score, maybe you just average the ones that are around it. So again, it's a matter of looking around you, doing this sort of social comparison with data to see how you should categorize a data point. To do this, I'm going to load a few packages, including caret, which is great for a number of approaches about predicting scores. And then I'm going to come down and I am actually going to start by setting a random seed. We're going to use some randomization in this process, and in order for that randomization to be repeatable, you set the seed and that makes it work the same each time. Now you can change the seed or you can remove the seed, but I'm going to set the seed here and you just pick a number in there. I'm using 333, but you can put anything that you want. And then I'm going to import our big five dataset. This is the big five personality factors,

It describes what some of the parameters are. And then it does these sample sizes where it's sampling from those of about 2,500 each time through. And then this is an interesting table. This says it tried 20 different values for K, and that is, it's going to look at the point in multidimensional space and how many of its neighbors should it look at. It's best to use odd numbers so you don't have ties, and you want to have at least five. And so it's trying five neighbors, then seven neighbors, all the way up to 43 neighbors. And what we're looking for is the value of K that has the highest accuracy of classification. These are proportions, and you can read these as percentages. So right here we have 0.56, that means 56% accurate classification. And you know, that's pretty impressive, although our categories are balanced. So you could just assign everybody at a high and be right 50% of the time.
>
> **[9:24](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/classifying-cases-with-k-nearest-neighbors?u=76281980&t=564)** So we also have kappa, which is an adjusted version of accuracy, which takes into consideration the randomness. So this is, you're going to get it right 50% of the time just at random. So this is an adjusted statistic, and you'll see that both of these peak when we get to 29, the accuracy is 57-67, and the kappa is 15-16. So that looks like it's our best value based on our training data. So I'm going to close that up. And so now we have that model saved and I can apply it to the test data. That's the 30% data I set off to the side. So what I do now is I'm going to predict the test set. I'm going to create a new variable called open_P, which means a predicted value for openness. And I'm going to use the predict function. It says, "Use the model that I saved earlier that I just called fit. And then this time, instead of the training data, use the test data." So we're going to run that. Runs pretty quickly. And then we want to get the accuracy. How well did this model predict openness on the test data? We're going to do that by creating a table where the actual or the true outcome comes from test, that's the object in the memory, and then open_T. And then we're going to do the predicted class, what the model said, where they were likely to be. And then we create a confusion matrix, which actually gives us a number of accuracy statistics. So let's print that out and then I'll zoom in on the console.
>
> **[10:57](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/classifying-cases-with-k-nearest-neighbors?u=76281980&t=657)** And you see here that of the actual categories of people who really were high on openness, that's this group right here, 267 were predicted to be high. 364 were predicted to be low. And then we also have, of those who were not high, that is they were low on openness, 163 were predicted to be high, 406 were predicted to be low. The overall accuracy is 56.08% and here's a 95% confidence interval as well as some other important information like sensitivity and specificity of the classification. And so this is a way of trying to categorize cases as to whether they would be high or low based on the model that we created with our testing data, using the K-nearest neighbors approach, and then applying it for validation purposes to our testing data and seeing exactly how well it performed.

You get the entire model with a picture, a series of yes-no decisions. And I want to show you how this works in R. And it would rotate all the way through as a way of balancing out the differences from one sample to another. Then we have the Accuracy of the models, as well as Kappa, which is a way of controlling for the inherent randomness in the data. If about 67% of my data is already female, I can predict that and be right a lot of the time.

>

I'll run that, and zoom in on it. And now you see the number of cases that were correctly classified or were misclassified, and we have 65% accuracy overall as well as the sensitivity and specificity. And so what this does is it takes our graphical analysis, the decision tree, that it based on doing this cross validated process on the training data, and apply it as, again, the gold standard for accuracy is to look at how well does it work on data that you did not train it on, which is what we did with the testing. It turns out decision trees are a very, very flexible approach. They can be used both for deciding about categorizations at the end, or scores on a quantitative variable. They can take almost any kind of input, and almost always, they give very easy to interpret models, which can then be saved and applied to new data sets. That's a way of getting useful and valid models to help you accomplish the goals that led to you gathering the data in the first place.

#### [Creating ensemble models with random forest classification](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-ensemble-models-with-random-forest-classification?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-ensemble-models-with-random-forest-classification?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] In our final demonstration of working in R to analyze and to classify data, I want to show you how to do something called a randomforest. Now, in a previous video I talked about [[Decision Trees]] where you take your data and you have a whole series of yes no decisions that split it off into these branches until you have a final model and it's able to portray it graphically, which is wonderful. A random forest is essentially a large collection of decision trees. It's also an introduction into what's known as ensemble modeling and what that is, instead of having just one model, use the wisdom of the crowds. You know the idea that two heads are better than one, use many models and combine them because typically the average predictions of several different models are going to be more accurate than the predictions of any one model. Now, to show you how this works, I'm going to load a few packages, including caret, which is used for decision trees and other predictive analyses. We'll do parallel, which allows for parallel processing and obviously the package randomforest to be doing this approach. So I'm going to load those packages and then we'll use the same data set and the same question that we had in the decision tree video. And I'm going to start by setting a random seed and then importing the big five data, which is what we had before. And what I'm bringing in here is gender and then five personality variables. Now I'm also sampling from the dataset. There are nearly 20,000 cases in this dataset,
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-ensemble-models-with-random-forest-classification?u=76281980&t=96)** but decision trees take a fair amount of time and doing lots of decision trees takes even longer. Again, I'm doing this on a relatively modest computer. I don't want you to have to wait half an hour. And so I'm reducing my total sample the 500 cases. If you have a faster computer, or if you have more patients, put it up to 1,000 or 10,000 or the full 18,000 and change. To get a feel for how this works on a large data set, what I'm going to do next is split the data into two parts. I'm going to create a training data set that has 70% of the data and a test data set that has all the [[Representational State Transfer (REST)|rest]] of it. And when I do that, 70% of 500 is 350 observations and the rest is 150. So it's a small amount. I'm going to be trying to categorize gender based on the five personality variables. So let's first see how many men and women we have in our dataset. This is the training dataset and we have more women than men. It's about 60% female, so it's about 50% more than we have men. And then let's get density plots for the five personality variables and then do them separately within each one for male and female respondents. So I'm going to run that command and then we'll [[Zoom]] in on that. So you can see that the distributions overlap a lot, but there are some noticeable differences. So this means potentially we can use this information to help classify people as being male or female.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-ensemble-models-with-random-forest-classification?u=76281980&t=192)** So let's then go to modeling the training data. I'm going to define a number of parameters to be used in creating our random forest. I say that I'm going to be using repeated cross validation. I want to do tenfold cross validation. So split the training data into 10 pieces and then use nine of those pieces to predict the scores in one piece and then rotate all the way through. We're going to do three sets of folds in terms of the number of tuning parameters. If you use random, it just sets it to the maximum value. And we will allow parallel processing. Obviously that's going to depend on the actual machine that you're using. So let's come here and save these parameters into control. You can see I now have that list over here, and then we can use those parameters to actually do the decision tree. This says I'm going to use the train functions 'cause we're training the model that we're going to use gender as predicted by the dot means all the other variables in the data set. We're using the training data set. rf means I want to use randomforests. Accuracy is the criterion that we're going to be using for evaluating these. There are several other choices for measures you can use. Accuracy is an easy one that works well, especially for when you have relatively balanced number of cases in your outcome categories. We'll use 15 levels of parameters, we'll do 800 trees and then this last one just links up to what we have here. So I'm going to run this and depending on how many cases you selected in your machine, this could take a few moments.
>
> **[4:48](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-ensemble-models-with-random-forest-classification?u=76281980&t=288)** So now that you've got this, let's take a look at the processing summary by simply calling the object rf, which I created to stand for randomforest. And we'll zoom in on that. And that gives us a little bit of information about the model. We had 350 samples, that's the cases or observation, five predictor variables. We had an outcome with two classes, male and female. And then we have information about the tenfold cross validation, the accuracy measures, the kappa, which is a version of accuracy that takes into consideration how likely you are to be at random and some other information. Now we can also plot the accuracy by the number of predictors. I'm going to run that one right here. This is not the tree, but what this is telling us in our 800 randomly generated decision trees, how many predictors did they each have? And it turns out a lot of them had just one, very few had just two, three, four and then down to five. So there were not many predictors involved. There were only five variables in the dataset, so we wouldn't go above five anyhow. One seems a little low, but that gives you an idea. It's choosing them at random and using cutoffs at random. That's part of the randomforests. But let's get the accuracy of the model on the training data. So we're going to do rf for randomforest that's where I saved all of this and pull out final model. And when we look in this, it doesn't give us the actual model because there were many different models that went into it, but it does give us the classifications and errors.
>
> **[6:23](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-ensemble-models-with-random-forest-classification?u=76281980&t=383)** Right here you see that, for instance, 59% of the males were misclassified as female. So there's a mistake. 21% of the females were correctly classified. That was a larger category. So when you average those two together, you get the OOB or out of bag estimate of error rate. That is, if you ignore the classification, what's the [[Probability]] that any randomly selected case is going to be classified correctly? Well, the error rate's about 35%. So we had about 65% categorized correctly. And the next thing we can do is we can actually plot the error rate by the number of trees by taking that same command and running it through plot. And what you see here, if I zoom in on this, this is the number of trees we ran up to 800 decision trees. Bottom in the green is the error rate for the females. Up here in the red is the error rate for the males. And you can see it's sort of converging. And the black is the out of bag, which is essentially a weighted average of either of the two. Now, the real test of all this is how well does it work on the other data, the test data that we set aside. So now we're going to get the predicted gender and we're going to use the test data. So now let's run that one now, that saves the new variable, and now we can get a table that shows us the accuracy of the actual gender versus the predicted gender. And a confusion matrix with a few [[Statistics]]. When I run that and we zoom in on it.
>
> **[7:58](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/creating-ensemble-models-with-random-forest-classification?u=76281980&t=478)** Now remember, this is a much smaller group. This is only 150 cases because the random process can take so long. Well, we have about 62% accuracy. Not very impressive, considering there's about 61% accuracy if you just say everyone's female. But that also lets you know that maybe the five variables I was using did not have a very strong association with our particular outcome. If you had a larger data set, which we could do if we simply were more patient, and if you had a larger set of predictive variables instead of five, maybe you have 50 or 500 or 5 million, and those have a stronger correlation with the outcome, then you'll find that randomforests can give you a much higher level of accuracy in prediction even with your testing data. But taken altogether, what this is is an example of how you can use a machine learning algorithm like decision trees, but multiplied many times. So you have many, many decision trees, 800 in this case, and then averaging the predictions across those to develop a model that you can use with data sets other than the ones you originally trained it on. That's the gold standard for generalizability and really utility of a model. And that's a great place to wrap up our discussion of data analysis, [[Data Modeling]], clustering, and classification in R.

We have a wide selection of courses here on [[LinkedIn]] learning that will help you with that. On the other hand, it's also good to deal with some of the very basic tools. I know we spend a lot of time on a programming language R, but understanding how spreadsheets work is going to be important because that's often how you will get data from clients and it's often how they'll want to have things back. And there's a lot of things for which spreadsheets are very, very good data tools. So, make sure you understand those. It makes it much easier to collaborate with your clients. Third, as much as I love R and am dedicated to it, learning about other programming languages like [[Python (Programming Language)|Python]], or SAS, or [[Stata]], or any others which might be well adapted to some unusual problems you could face and makes it easier, again, to collaborate with other people. And then, finally, getting a better understanding of business strategy. Remember, data isn't there just because you need data and you want to work with data. Data helps you solve problems. It helps you find the meaningful signal in the noise that's all around you. And understanding how your insights can be acted upon by your clients and understanding the business environment
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/complete-guide-to-r-wrangling-visualizing-and-modeling-data/next-steps?u=76281980&t=94)** that they're working in is going to give your work much, much greater impact. Now, one way to build on all of this is to start attending user groups or meetup groups or collaborating with people who work in fields that are important to you. But more than anything, just get out there and see what you can do. Get some data on a topic that's important to you and start exploring to see how you can use R to get insight and make your worth both exciting and more engaging. Thank you so much for joining me and best of luck in getting the critical insights that you need from your data.
